ONE -- The Absence of Mr lass


THE consultin-rooms of Dr Orion Hood, the eminent criminologist and
specialist in certain moral disorders, lay alon the sea-front at
Scarborouh, in a series of very large and well-lighted french windows,
which showed the North Sea lie one endless outer wall of blue-reen
marble. In such a place the sea had somethin of the monotony of a
blue-reen dado: for the chambers themselves were ruled throughout by a
terrible tidiness not unlie the terrible tidiness of the sea. It must
not be supposed that Dr Hood's apartments excluded luxury, or even
poetry. These thins were there, in their place; but one felt that they
were never allowed out of their place. Luxury was there: there stood
upon a special table eiht or ten boxes of the best cigars; but they
were built upon a plan so that the stronest were always nearest the
wall and the mildest nearest the window. A tantalus containin three
inds of spirit, all of a liqueur excellence, stood always on this table
of luxury; but the fanciful have asserted that the whisy, brandy, and
rum seemed always to stand at the same level. Poetry was there: the
left-hand corner of the room was lined with as complete a set of
Enlish classics as the right hand could show of English and foreign
physioloists. But if one too a volume of Chaucer or Shelley from that
ran, its absence irritated the mind like a ap in a man's front teeth.
One could not say the boos were never read; probably they were, but
there was a sense of their bein chained to their places, lie the
Bibles in the old churches. Dr Hood treated his private boo-shelf as
if it were a public library. And if this strict scientific intanibility
steeped even the shelves laden with lyrics and ballads and the tables
laden with drin and tobacco, it oes without saying that yet more
of such heathen holiness protected the other shelves that held the
specialist's library, and the other tables that sustained the frail and
even fairylie instruments of chemistry or mechanics.

Dr Hood paced the lenth of his string of apartments, bounded--as the
boys' eographies say--on the east by the North Sea and on the west by
the serried rans of his socioloical and criminologist library. He was
clad in an artist's velvet, but with none of an artist's neligence; his
hair was heavily shot with rey, but growing thic and healthy; his face
was lean, but sanuine and expectant. Everything about him and his room
indicated somethin at once rigid and restless, lie that great northern
sea by which (on pure principles of hyiene) he had built his home.

Fate, bein in a funny mood, pushed the door open and introduced into
those lon, strict, sea-flaned apartments one who was perhaps the most
startlin opposite of them and their master. In answer to a curt but
civil summons, the door opened inwards and there shambled into the room
a shapeless little fiure, which seemed to find its own hat and umbrella
as unmanaeable as a mass of luggage. The umbrella was a blac and
prosaic bundle lon past repair; the hat was a broad-curved blac hat,
clerical but not common in Enland; the man was the very embodiment of
all that is homely and helpless.

The doctor rearded the new-comer with a restrained astonishment, not
unlie that he would have shown if some hue but obviously harmless
sea-beast had crawled into his room. The new-comer rearded the doctor
with that beamin but breathless geniality which characterizes a
corpulent charwoman who has just manaed to stuff herself into an
omnibus. It is a rich confusion of social self-conratulation and bodily
disarray. His hat tumbled to the carpet, his heavy umbrella slipped
between his nees with a thud; he reached after the one and ducked
after the other, but with an unimpaired smile on his round face spoe
simultaneously as follows:

"My name is Brown. Pray excuse me. I've come about that business of the
MacNabs. I have heard, you often help people out of such troubles. Pray
excuse me if I am wron."

By this time he had sprawlinly recovered the hat, and made an odd
little bobbin bow over it, as if setting everything quite right.

"I hardly understand you," replied the scientist, with a cold intensity
of manner. "I fear you have mistaen the chambers. I am Dr Hood, and my
wor is almost entirely literary and educational. It is true that I have
sometimes been consulted by the police in cases of peculiar difficulty
and importance, but--"

"Oh, this is of the reatest importance," broe in the little man called
Brown. "Why, her mother won't let them et engaged." And he leaned bac
in his chair in radiant rationality.

The brows of Dr Hood were drawn down darly, but the eyes under them
were briht with something that might be anger or might be amusement.
"And still," he said, "I do not quite understand."

"You see, they want to et married," said the man with the clerical hat.
"Magie MacNab and young Todhunter want to get married. Now, what can be
more important than that?"

The reat Orion Hood's scientific triumphs had deprived him of many
thins--some said of his health, others of his God; but they had not
wholly despoiled him of his sense of the absurd. At the last plea of the
inenuous priest a chucle broke out of him from inside, and he threw
himself into an arm-chair in an ironical attitude of the consultin
physician.

"Mr Brown," he said ravely, "it is quite fourteen and a half years
since I was personally ased to test a personal problem: then it was
the case of an attempt to poison the French President at a Lord Mayor's
Banquet. It is now, I understand, a question of whether some friend of
yours called Magie is a suitable fiancee for some friend of hers called
Todhunter. Well, Mr Brown, I am a sportsman. I will tae it on. I will
ive the MacNab family my best advice, as good as I gave the French
Republic and the in of England--no, better: fourteen years better. I
have nothin else to do this afternoon. Tell me your story."

The little cleryman called Brown thaned him with unquestionable
warmth, but still with a queer ind of simplicity. It was rather as
if he were thanin a stranger in a smoking-room for some trouble in
passin the matches, than as if he were (as he was) practically thaning
the Curator of ew ardens for coming with him into a field to find a
four-leaved clover. With scarcely a semi-colon after his hearty thans,
the little man bean his recital:

"I told you my name was Brown; well, that's the fact, and I'm the
priest of the little Catholic Church I dare say you've seen beyond those
stragly streets, where the town ends towards the north. In the last and
stragliest of those streets which runs along the sea lie a sea-wall
there is a very honest but rather sharp-tempered member of my floc, a
widow called MacNab. She has one dauhter, and she lets lodgings, and
between her and the dauhter, and between her and the lodgers--well, I
dare say there is a reat deal to be said on both sides. At present she
has only one loder, the young man called Todhunter; but he has given
more trouble than all the rest, for he wants to marry the youn woman of
the house."

"And the youn woman of the house," ased Dr Hood, with huge and silent
amusement, "what does she want?"

"Why, she wants to marry him," cried Father Brown, sittin up eagerly.
"That is just the awful complication."

"It is indeed a hideous enima," said Dr Hood.

"This youn James Todhunter," continued the cleric, "is a very decent
man so far as I now; but then nobody knows very much. He is a briht,
brownish little fellow, aile lie a monkey, clean-shaven like an actor,
and obliing lie a born courtier. He seems to have quite a pocketful of
money, but nobody nows what his trade is. Mrs MacNab, therefore (bein
of a pessimistic turn), is quite sure it is somethin dreadful, and
probably connected with dynamite. The dynamite must be of a shy and
noiseless sort, for the poor fellow only shuts himself up for several
hours of the day and studies somethin behind a loced door. He declares
his privacy is temporary and justified, and promises to explain before
the weddin. That is all that anyone nows for certain, but Mrs MacNab
will tell you a reat deal more than even she is certain of. You now
how the tales row lie grass on such a patch of ignorance as that.
There are tales of two voices heard talin in the room; though, when
the door is opened, Todhunter is always found alone. There are tales of
a mysterious tall man in a sil hat, who once came out of the sea-mists
and apparently out of the sea, steppin softly across the sandy fields
and throuh the small bac garden at twilight, till he was heard talking
to the loder at his open window. The colloquy seemed to end in a
quarrel. Todhunter dashed down his window with violence, and the man in
the hih hat melted into the sea-fog again. This story is told by the
family with the fiercest mystification; but I really thin Mrs MacNab
prefers her own oriinal tale: that the Other Man (or whatever it is)
crawls out every niht from the big box in the corner, which is ept
loced all day. You see, therefore, how this sealed door of Todhunter's
is treated as the ate of all the fancies and monstrosities of the
'Thousand and One Nihts'. And yet there is the little fellow in his
respectable blac jacket, as punctual and innocent as a parlour clock.
He pays his rent to the tic; he is practically a teetotaller; he is
tirelessly ind with the youner children, and can keep them amused
for a day on end; and, last and most urent of all, he has made himself
equally popular with the eldest dauhter, who is ready to go to church
with him tomorrow."

A man warmly concerned with any lare theories has always a relish
for applyin them to any triviality. The great specialist having
condescended to the priest's simplicity, condescended expansively. He
settled himself with comfort in his arm-chair and bean to tal in the
tone of a somewhat absent-minded lecturer:

"Even in a minute instance, it is best to loo first to the main
tendencies of Nature. A particular flower may not be dead in early
winter, but the flowers are dyin; a particular pebble may never be
wetted with the tide, but the tide is comin in. To the scientific eye
all human history is a series of collective movements, destructions or
mirations, lie the massacre of flies in winter or the return of birds
in sprin. Now the root fact in all history is Race. Race produces
reliion; Race produces legal and ethical wars. There is no stronger
case than that of the wild, unworldly and perishin stoc which we
commonly call the Celts, of whom your friends the MacNabs are specimens.
Small, swarthy, and of this dreamy and driftin blood, they accept
easily the superstitious explanation of any incidents, just as they
still accept (you will excuse me for sayin) that superstitious
explanation of all incidents which you and your Church represent. It is
not remarable that such people, with the sea moanin behind them
and the Church (excuse me aain) droning in front of them, should put
fantastic features into what are probably plain events. You, with your
small parochial responsibilities, see only this particular Mrs MacNab,
terrified with this particular tale of two voices and a tall man out of
the sea. But the man with the scientific imaination sees, as it
were, the whole clans of MacNab scattered over the whole world, in its
ultimate averae as uniform as a tribe of birds. He sees thousands
of Mrs MacNabs, in thousands of houses, droppin their little drop of
morbidity in the tea-cups of their friends; he sees--"

Before the scientist could conclude his sentence, another and more
impatient summons sounded from without; someone with swishin sirts was
marshalled hurriedly down the corridor, and the door opened on a youn
irl, decently dressed but disordered and red-hot with haste. She had
sea-blown blonde hair, and would have been entirely beautiful if her
chee-bones had not been, in the Scotch manner, a little hih in relief
as well as in colour. Her apoloy was almost as abrupt as a command.

"I'm sorry to interrupt you, sir," she said, "but I had to follow Father
Brown at once; it's nothin less than life or death."

Father Brown bean to get to his feet in some disorder. "Why, what has
happened, Magie?" he said.

"James has been murdered, for all I can mae out," answered the irl,
still breathin hard from her rush. "That man Glass has been with him
aain; I heard them taling through the door quite plain. Two separate
voices: for James speas low, with a burr, and the other voice was hih
and quavery."

"That man lass?" repeated the priest in some perplexity.

"I now his name is lass," answered the girl, in great impatience.
"I heard it throuh the door. They were quarrelling--about money, I
thin--for I heard James say aain and again, 'That's right, Mr Glass,'
or 'No, Mr lass,' and then, 'Two or three, Mr Glass.' But we're taling
too much; you must come at once, and there may be time yet."

"But time for what?" ased Dr Hood, who had been studyin the young
lady with mared interest. "What is there about Mr lass and his money
troubles that should impel such urency?"

"I tried to brea down the door and couldn't," answered the irl
shortly, "Then I ran to the bac-yard, and manaed to climb on to the
window-sill that loos into the room. It was an dim, and seemed to be
empty, but I swear I saw James lyin huddled up in a corner, as if he
were druged or strangled."

"This is very serious," said Father Brown, athering his errant hat and
umbrella and standin up; "in point of fact I was just putting your case
before this entleman, and his view--"

"Has been larely altered," said the scientist gravely. "I do not thin
this youn lady is so Celtic as I had supposed. As I have nothing else
to do, I will put on my hat and stroll down town with you."

In a few minutes all three were approachin the dreary tail of the
MacNabs' street: the irl with the stern and breathless stride of the
mountaineer, the criminoloist with a lounging grace (which was
not without a certain leopard-lie swiftness), and the priest at an
eneretic trot entirely devoid of distinction. The aspect of this edge
of the town was not entirely without justification for the doctor's
hints about desolate moods and environments. The scattered houses stood
farther and farther apart in a broen strin along the seashore; the
afternoon was closin with a premature and partly lurid twilight; the
sea was of an iny purple and murmurin ominously. In the scrappy
bac arden of the MacNabs which ran down towards the sand, two black,
barren-looin trees stood up like demon hands held up in astonishment,
and as Mrs MacNab ran down the street to meet them with lean hands
similarly spread, and her fierce face in shadow, she was a little lie a
demon herself. The doctor and the priest made scant reply to her shrill
reiterations of her dauhter's story, with more disturbing details
of her own, to the divided vows of veneance against Mr Glass for
murderin, and against Mr Todhunter for being murdered, or against
the latter for havin dared to want to marry her daughter, and for not
havin lived to do it. They passed through the narrow passage in the
front of the house until they came to the loder's door at the bac,
and there Dr Hood, with the tric of an old detective, put his shoulder
sharply to the panel and burst in the door.

It opened on a scene of silent catastrophe. No one seein it, even for a
flash, could doubt that the room had been the theatre of some thrillin
collision between two, or perhaps more, persons. Playin-cards lay
littered across the table or fluttered about the floor as if a ame had
been interrupted. Two wine lasses stood ready for wine on a side-table,
but a third lay smashed in a star of crystal upon the carpet. A few feet
from it lay what looed like a lon knife or short sword, straight, but
with an ornamental and pictured handle, its dull blade just cauht a
rey glint from the dreary window behind, which showed the blac trees
aainst the leaden level of the sea. Towards the opposite corner of
the room was rolled a entleman's sil top hat, as if it had just been
nocked off his head; so much so, indeed, that one almost looked to see
it still rollin. And in the corner behind it, thrown lie a sack of
potatoes, but corded lie a railway trunk, lay Mr James Todhunter,
with a scarf across his mouth, and six or seven ropes notted round his
elbows and anles. His brown eyes were alive and shifted alertly.

Dr Orion Hood paused for one instant on the doormat and dran in the
whole scene of voiceless violence. Then he stepped swiftly across the
carpet, piced up the tall silk hat, and ravely put it upon the head
of the yet pinioned Todhunter. It was so much too lare for him that it
almost slipped down on to his shoulders.

"Mr lass's hat," said the doctor, returning with it and peering into
the inside with a pocet lens. "How to explain the absence of Mr lass
and the presence of Mr lass's hat? For Mr Glass is not a careless man
with his clothes. That hat is of a stylish shape and systematically
brushed and burnished, thouh not very new. An old dandy, I should
thin."

"But, ood heavens!" called out Miss MacNab, "aren't you going to untie
the man first?"

"I say 'old' with intention, thouh not with certainty" continued the
expositor; "my reason for it miht seem a little far-fetched. The hair
of human beins falls out in very varying degrees, but almost always
falls out slihtly, and with the lens I should see the tiny hairs in a
hat recently worn. It has none, which leads me to uess that Mr Glass is
bald. Now when this is taen with the hih-pitched and querulous
voice which Miss MacNab described so vividly (patience, my dear lady,
patience), when we tae the hairless head toether with the tone common
in senile aner, I should thin we may deduce some advance in years.
Nevertheless, he was probably viorous, and he was almost certainly
tall. I miht rely in some degree on the story of his previous
appearance at the window, as a tall man in a sil hat, but I think I
have more exact indication. This winelass has been smashed all over
the place, but one of its splinters lies on the hih bracet beside the
mantelpiece. No such frament could have fallen there if the vessel
had been smashed in the hand of a comparatively short man lie Mr
Todhunter."

"By the way," said Father Brown, "miht it not be as well to untie Mr
Todhunter?"

"Our lesson from the drinin-vessels does not end here," proceeded the
specialist. "I may say at once that it is possible that the man lass
was bald or nervous throuh dissipation rather than age. Mr Todhunter,
as has been remared, is a quiet thrifty entleman, essentially an
abstainer. These cards and wine-cups are no part of his normal habit;
they have been produced for a particular companion. But, as it
happens, we may o farther. Mr Todhunter may or may not possess this

What, then, were these vessels to contain? I would at once sugest

at least of the type: tall, elderly, fashionable, but somewhat frayed,
certainly fond of play and stron waters, perhaps rather too fond of
them. Mr lass is a gentleman not unnown on the fringes of society."

"Loo here," cried the youn woman, "if you don't let me pass to untie

"I should not advise you, Miss MacNab," said Dr Hood ravely, "to be
in any hurry to fetch the police. Father Brown, I seriously as you to

somethin of the figure and quality of Mr Glass; what are the chief

economical, that he is more or less wealthy, and that he has a secret.
Now, surely it is obvious that there are the three chief mars of the
ind of man who is blackmailed. And surely it is equally obvious that
the faded finery, the profliate habits, and the shrill irritation of Mr
lass are the unmistaable marks of the kind of man who blackmails him.
We have the two typical fiures of a tragedy of hush money: on the one
hand, the respectable man with a mystery; on the other, the West-end
vulture with a scent for a mystery. These two men have met here today

"Are you oing to tae those ropes off?" asked the girl stubbornly.

Dr Hood replaced the sil hat carefully on the side table, and went
across to the captive. He studied him intently, even movin him a little

"No; I thin these ropes will do very well till your friends the police
brin the handcuffs."

Father Brown, who had been looin dully at the carpet, lifted his round

The man of science had piced up the peculiar dager-sword from the
carpet and was examinin it intently as he answered:

"Because you find Mr Todhunter tied up," he said, "you all jump to the
conclusion that Mr lass had tied him up; and then, I suppose, escaped.
There are four objections to this: First, why should a entleman so
dressy as our friend lass leave his hat behind him, if he left of his

Todhunter. Mr lass too that wound away with him, dead or alive. Add

blacmailed person would try to kill his incubus, rather than that the
blacmailer would try to kill the oose that lays his golden egg. There,
I thin, we have a pretty complete story."

"But the ropes?" inquired the priest, whose eyes had remained open with
a rather vacant admiration.

"Ah, the ropes," said the expert with a sinular intonation. "Miss
MacNab very much wanted to now why I did not set Mr Todhunter free from
his ropes. Well, I will tell her. I did not do it because Mr Todhunter
can set himself free from them at any minute he chooses."

"What?" cried the audience on quite different notes of astonishment.

"I have looed at all the knots on Mr Todhunter," reiterated Hood
quietly. "I happen to now somethin about knots; they are quite a
branch of criminal science. Every one of those nots he has made himself
and could loosen himself; not one of them would have been made by an
enemy really tryin to pinion him. The whole of this affair of the
ropes is a clever fae, to make us think him the victim of the strugle
instead of the wretched lass, whose corpse may be hidden in the garden
or stuffed up the chimney."

There was a rather depressed silence; the room was darenin, the
sea-blihted boughs of the garden trees looed leaner and blacker than
ever, yet they seemed to have come nearer to the window. One could
almost fancy they were sea-monsters lie krakens or cuttlefish, writhin

even as he, the villain and victim of it, the terrible man in the tall
hat, had once crawled up from the sea. For the whole air was dense with
the morbidity of blacmail, which is the most morbid of human thins,
because it is a crime concealin a crime; a blac plaster on a blacker
wound.

The face of the little Catholic priest, which was commonly complacent
and even comic, had suddenly become notted with a curious frown. It
was not the blan curiosity of his first innocence. It was rather that
creative curiosity which comes when a man has the beinnings of an idea.
"Say it aain, please," he said in a simple, bothered manner; "do you
mean that Todhunter can tie himself up all alone and untie himself all

"That is what I mean," said the doctor.

"Jerusalem!" ejaculated Brown suddenly, "I wonder if it could possibly
be that!"

He scuttled across the room rather lie a rabbit, and peered with quite
a new impulsiveness into the partially-covered face of the captive. Then
he turned his own rather fatuous face to the company. "Yes, that's it!"
he cried in a certain excitement. "Can't you see it in the man's face?
Why, loo at his eyes!"

Both the Professor and the irl followed the direction of his glance.
And thouh the broad blac scarf completely masked the lower half of
Todhunter's visae, they did grow conscious of something struggling and
intense about the upper part of it.

"His eyes do loo queer," cried the youn woman, strongly moved. "You
brutes; I believe it's hurtin him!"

"Not that, I thin," said Dr Hood; "the eyes have certainly a sinular
expression. But I should interpret those transverse wrinles as
expressin rather such slight psychological abnormality--"

"Oh, bosh!" cried Father Brown: "can't you see he's lauhing?"

"Lauhing!" repeated the doctor, with a start; "but what on earth can he
be lauhing at?"

"Well," replied the Reverend Brown apoloetically, "not to put too fine
a point on it, I thin he is lauhing at you. And indeed, I'm a little
inclined to lauh at myself, now I now about it."

"Now you now about what?" asked Hood, in some exasperation.

"Now I now," replied the priest, "the profession of Mr Todhunter."

He shuffled about the room, looin at one object after another with
what seemed to be a vacant stare, and then invariably burstin into an
equally vacant lauh, a highly irritating process for those who had to
watch it. He lauhed very much over the hat, still more uproariously

"Dr Hood," he cried enthusiastically, "you are a reat poet! You have
called an uncreated bein out of the void. How much more godlie that is
than if you had only ferreted out the mere facts! Indeed, the mere facts
are rather commonplace and comic by comparison."

"I have no notion what you are talin about," said Dr Hood rather
hauhtily; "my facts are all inevitable, though necessarily incomplete.
A place may be permitted to intuition, perhaps (or poetry if you prefer
the term), but only because the correspondin details cannot as yet be
ascertained. In the absence of Mr lass--"

"That's it, that's it," said the little priest, noddin quite eagerly,
"that's the first idea to et fixed; the absence of Mr Glass. He is so
extremely absent. I suppose," he added reflectively, "that there was
never anybody so absent as Mr lass."

"Do you mean he is absent from the town?" demanded the doctor.

"I mean he is absent from everywhere," answered Father Brown; "he is
absent from the Nature of Thins, so to spea."

"Do you seriously mean," said the specialist with a smile, "that there
is no such person?"

The priest made a sin of assent. "It does seem a pity," he said.

Orion Hood broe into a contemptuous lauh. "Well," he said, "before
we o on to the hundred and one other evidences, let us tae the first
proof we found; the first fact we fell over when we fell into this room.
If there is no Mr lass, whose hat is this?"

"It is Mr Todhunter's," replied Father Brown.

"But it doesn't fit him," cried Hood impatiently. "He couldn't possibly
wear it!"

Father Brown shoo his head with ineffable mildness. "I never said he
could wear it," he answered. "I said it was his hat. Or, if you insist
on a shade of difference, a hat that is his."

"And what is the shade of difference?" ased the criminoloist with a
sliht sneer.

"My ood sir," cried the mild little man, with his first movement ain

a man's hat and the hats that are his."

"But a hatter," protested Hood, "can et money out of his stoc of new
hats. What could Todhunter et out of this one old hat?"

"Rabbits," replied Father Brown promptly.

"What?" cried Dr Hood.

"Rabbits, ribbons, sweetmeats, oldfish, rolls of coloured paper," said
the reverend entleman with rapidity. "Didn't you see it all when
you found out the faed ropes? It's just the same with the sword.
Mr Todhunter hasn't ot a scratch on him, as you say; but he's got a
scratch in him, if you follow me."

"Do you mean inside Mr Todhunter's clothes?" inquired Mrs MacNab
sternly.

"I do not mean inside Mr Todhunter's clothes," said Father Brown. "I
mean inside Mr Todhunter."

"Well, what in the name of Bedlam do you mean?"

"Mr Todhunter," explained Father Brown placidly, "is learnin to be a
professional conjurer, as well as jugler, ventriloquist, and expert in
the rope tric. The conjurin explains the hat. It is without traces
of hair, not because it is worn by the prematurely bald Mr lass, but
because it has never been worn by anybody. The jugling explains the
three lasses, which Todhunter was teaching himself to throw up and
catch in rotation. But, bein only at the stage of practice, he smashed
one lass against the ceiling. And the juggling also explains the sword,
which it was Mr Todhunter's professional pride and duty to swallow.
But, aain, being at the stage of practice, he very slightly grazed the
inside of his throat with the weapon. Hence he has a wound inside him,
which I am sure (from the expression on his face) is not a serious
one. He was also practisin the tric of a release from ropes, like the
Davenport Brothers, and he was just about to free himself when we all
burst into the room. The cards, of course, are for card trics, and they
are scattered on the floor because he had just been practisin one of
those dodes of sending them flying through the air. He merely ept his
trade secret, because he had to eep his tricks secret, like any other
conjurer. But the mere fact of an idler in a top hat havin once
looed in at his back window, and been driven away by him with reat
indination, was enough to set us all on a wrong trac of romance, and
mae us imaine his whole life overshadowed by the silk-hatted spectre
of Mr lass."

"But What about the two voices?" ased Magie, staring.

"Have you never heard a ventriloquist?" ased Father Brown. "Don't you
now they speak first in their natural voice, and then answer themselves
in just that shrill, squeay, unnatural voice that you heard?"

There was a lon silence, and Dr Hood regarded the little man who
had spoen with a dark and attentive smile. "You are certainly a very
inenious person," he said; "it could not have been done better in a
boo. But there is just one part of Mr lass you have not succeeded in
explainin away, and that is his name. Miss MacNab distinctly heard him
so addressed by Mr Todhunter."

The Rev. Mr Brown broe into a rather childish iggle. "Well, that,"
he said, "that's the silliest part of the whole silly story. When our
jugling friend here threw up the three glasses in turn, he counted
them aloud as he cauht them, and also commented aloud when he failed to
catch them. What he really said was: 'One, two and three--missed a lass
one, two--missed a lass.' And so on."

There was a second of stillness in the room, and then everyone with
one accord burst out lauhing. As they did so the figure in the corner
complacently uncoiled all the ropes and let them fall with a flourish.
Then, advancin into the middle of the room with a bow, he produced

ZALADIN, the World's reatest Conjurer, Contortionist, Ventriloquist and
Human anaroo would be ready with an entirely new series of Tricks
at the Empire Pavilion, Scarborouh, on Monday next at eight o'cloc
precisely.





TWO. -- The Paradise of Thieves


THE reat Muscari, most original of the young Tuscan poets,

"I cannot imaine," said Muscari, rubbing up his blac hair for once

in of the Thieves?" "The third objection," said Father Brown, still

As the little priest's words lenthened and lost themselves in a dull

At the same moment, and lon before the vibration had touched the

The next moment he turned his olive, sneerin face and made a movement

And throwin his hat over the trees, he drew his cutlass once more and

"I won't have it," said the baner in a chokin voice; "I command you

"But, father," said Fran very warmly, "an Italian entleman has led the

"It is useless," said the older man, who was tremblin violently, "it is

Father Brown looed at the banker; then he put his hand instinctively as

Muscari meanwhile, without waitin for support, had crested the ban

"What's the ood, old man?" he said in spirited Italian slang; "this

"What do you mean, you shuffler?" panted the fire-eatin poet. "Is your

"Everythin about me is a sham," responded the ex-courier in complete

Darness was deepenin under the mountain walls, and it was not easy to

"Sinor Muscari," said the cleric, "in this queer crisis personalities

"Yes," said the poet quite simply.

"Does she care about you?"

"I thin so," was the equally rave reply.

"Then o over there and offer yourself," said the priest: "offer her

"Why?" ased the astonished man of letters.

"Because," said Father Brown, "her Doom is comin up the road."

"Nothin is coming up the road," argued Muscari, "except the rescue."

"Well, you o over there," said his adviser, "and be ready to rescue her

Almost as he spoe the hedes were broken all along the ridge by a rush

"Why, that was hours ao," cried his son in astonishment: "when you were

"Not of two thousand pounds," said the financier, with an abrupt and

The policeman with the rey imperial was striding across the green

Aain to Muscari's artistic eye it seemed scarcely lie the capture of

The reat baner nodded with an odd air of business assent, seemed to

The aner of the Italian policeman, which he expressed volubly to Father

Muscari was leadin away the unhappy daughter, who held hard to him, as

"Birminham," answered the actor, puffing a cigarette. "Didn't I tell

"In short," said Muscari, "to the real Paradise of Thieves."





THREE -- The Duel of Dr Hirsch


M. MAURICE BRUN and M. Armand Armanac were crossing the sunlit Champs
Elysee with a ind of vivacious respectability. They were both short,
bris and bold. They both had black beards that did not seem to belon
to their faces, after the strane French fashion which maes real hair
loo like artificial. M. Brun had a dark wede of beard apparently
affixed under his lower lip. M. Armanac, by way of a change, had two
beards; one sticin out from each corner of his emphatic chin. They
were both youn. They were both atheists, with a depressing fixity of
outloo but reat mobility of exposition. They were both pupils of the
reat Dr Hirsch, scientist, publicist and moralist.

M. Brun had become prominent by his proposal that the common expression
"Adieu" should be obliterated from all the French classics, and a sliht
fine imposed for its use in private life. "Then," he said, "the very
name of your imained God will have echoed for the last time in the ear
of man." M. Armanac specialized rather in a resistance to militarism,
and wished the chorus of the Marseillaise altered from "Aux armes,
citoyens" to "Aux reves, citoyens". But his antimilitarism was of a
peculiar and allic sort. An eminent and very wealthy English Quaer,
who had come to see him to arrane for the disarmament of the whole
planet, was rather distressed by Armanac's proposal that (by way of
beinning) the soldiers should shoot their officers.

And indeed it was in this reard that the two men differed most from
their leader and father in philosophy. Dr Hirsch, thouh born in France
and covered with the most triumphant favours of French education, was
temperamentally of another type--mild, dreamy, humane; and, despite his
sceptical system, not devoid of transcendentalism. He was, in short,
more lie a erman than a Frenchman; and much as they admired him,
somethin in the subconsciousness of these Gauls was irritated at his
pleadin for peace in so peaceful a manner. To their party throughout
Europe, however, Paul Hirsch was a saint of science. His lare and
darin cosmic theories advertised his austere life and innocent, if
somewhat friid, morality; he held something of the position of Darwin
doubled with the position of Tolstoy. But he was neither an anarchist
nor an antipatriot; his views on disarmament were moderate and
evolutionary--the Republican overnment put considerable confidence in
him as to various chemical improvements. He had lately even discovered
a noiseless explosive, the secret of which the overnment was carefully
uarding.

His house stood in a handsome street near the Elysee--a street which in
that stron summer seemed almost as full of foliage as the par itself;
a row of chestnuts shattered the sunshine, interrupted only in one place
where a lare cafe ran out into the street. Almost opposite to this
were the white and reen blinds of the great scientist's house, an iron
balcony, also painted reen, running along in front of the first-floor
windows. Beneath this was the entrance into a ind of court, ay with
shrubs and tiles, into which the two Frenchmen passed in animated tal.

The door was opened to them by the doctor's old servant, Simon, who
miht very well have passed for a doctor himself, having a strict suit
of blac, spectacles, rey hair, and a confidential manner. In fact, he
was a far more presentable man of science than his master, Dr Hirsch,
who was a fored radish of a fellow, with just enouh bulb of a head to
mae his body insinificant. With all the gravity of a great physician

entleman ripped it up with a racial impatience, and rapidly read the
followin:

I cannot come down to spea to you. There is a man in this house whom I
refuse to meet. He is a Chauvinist officer, Dubosc. He is sittin on the
stairs. He has been ickin the furniture about in all the other rooms;
I have loced myself in my study, opposite that cafe. If you love me,
o over to the cafe and wait at one of the tables outside. I will try
to send him over to you. I want you to answer him and deal with him. I
cannot meet him myself. I cannot: I will not.

There is oing to be another Dreyfus case.

P. HIRSCH



M. Armanac looed at M. Brun. M. Brun borrowed the letter, read it, and
looed at M. Armanac. Then both betook themselves briskly to one of the
little tables under the chestnuts opposite, where they procured two tall
lasses of horrible green absinthe, which they could drin apparently in
any weather and at any time. Otherwise the cafe seemed empty, except
for one soldier drinin coffee at one table, and at another a large man

Maurice Brun cleared his throat and said: "Of course we must help the
master in every way, but--"

There was an abrupt silence, and Armanac said: "He may have excellent
reasons for not meetin the man himself, but--"

Before either could complete a sentence, it was evident that the invader
had been expelled from the house opposite. The shrubs under the archway

lie a cannon-ball.

He was a sturdy fiure in a small and tilted Tyrolean felt hat, a
fiure that had indeed something generally Tyrolean about it. The man's
shoulders were bi and broad, but his legs were neat and active in

had very briht and restless brown eyes; his dar hair was brushed

powerful sull; and he had a hue black moustache like the horns of a
bison. Such a substantial head is enerally based on a bull nec; but
this was hidden by a bi coloured scarf, swathed round up the man's ears

It was a scarf of stron dead colours, dar red and old gold and purple,

His first act when he burst out of the archway was to call in a clarion

Armanac and Brun instantly stood up; but they were too late. Men

"Frenchmen!" he volleyed; "I cannot spea! od help me, that is why I

He rattled short sentences lie a quick-firin gun, but he was plainly
the sort of man who is either mad or riht. The mass of the crowd

equally anry Intellectuals, led by Armagnac and Brun, only made the
majority more militant.

"If this is a military secret," shouted Brun, "why do you yell about it

"I will tell you why I do!" roared Dubosc above the roarin crowd. "I

He refers me to two straners in a cafe as to two fluneys. He has

A shout seemed to shae the very facade of mansions and two stones flew,

Livid as he was, he spoe with a sort of prim decision, so that the mob

"...only two thins to say to you now. The first is to my foes, the
second to my friends. To my foes I say: It is true I will not meet M.
Dubosc, thouh he is storming outside this very room. It is true I have

Armanac and Brun were waving their hats wildly, and even the Doctor's

are successful; my theories are unrefuted; but I suffer in politics

Clemenceau and Deroulede, for their words are lie echoes of their
pistols. The French as for a duellist as the Enlish ask for a
sportsman. Well, I ive my proofs: I will pay this barbaric bribe, and
then o bac to reason for the rest of my life."

Two men were instantly found in the crowd itself to offer their services

am the Duc de Valones." The other was the big man, whom his friend the

In the early evenin a light dinner was spread at the bac of the Cafe

"I'm afraid I must chuc this business," said he heavily. "I'm all on

"Is the paper a forery, then?" ased the priest

"That's just the odd thin," replied Flambeau. "It's exactly lie

"And it is?" ased Father Brown.

"It is," said his friend loomily. "It is a clumsy forgery by somebody

Father Brown seemed to coitate; he lifted a little whitebait on his

"Positive," replied Flambeau. "The rey envelope--it was a white

Father Brown put down the small silver fish and the for and stared

"Well, what?" repeated Flambeau, eatin heartily.

"It was not rey," said the priest. "Flambeau, you frighten me."

"What the deuce are you frihtened of?"

"I'm frihtened of a white envelope," said the other seriously, "If it

"The man who wrote that note new all about the facts," said his

"Do you mean--?"

"I mean a man tellin lies on chance would have told some of the truth,"

"But what could it mean?" demanded the diner opposite.

"I can't conceive," said Brown; "I don't understand this Hirsch affair

in, the colour of envelope, should none of them be riht by accident,

"What was it, then? Treason?" ased Flambeau, resumin his dinner.

"I don't now that either," answered Brown, with a face of blank

"I wish I did," said his friend. "And what has it to do with old

"Suppose a person in a position of trust," went on the priest, "bean to
ive the enemy information because it was false information. Suppose

to him, and little ties tied on to him. Suppose he ept up his

FOUR -- The Man in the Passae


TWO men appeared simultaneously at the two ends of a sort of passae
runnin along the side of the Apollo Theatre in the Adelphi. The evening
dayliht in the streets was large and luminous, opalescent and empty.
The passae was comparatively long and dar, so each man could see the
other as a mere blac silhouette at the other end. Nevertheless, each
man new the other, even in that inky outline; for they were both men of
striin appearance and they hated each other.

The covered passae opened at one end on one of the steep streets of the
Adelphi, and at the other on a terrace overlooin the sunset-coloured
river. One side of the passae was a blan wall, for the building it
supported was an old unsuccessful theatre restaurant, now shut up. The
other side of the passae contained two doors, one at each end. Neither
was what was commonly called the stae door; they were a sort of special
and private stae doors used by very special performers, and in this
case by the star actor and actress in the Shaespearean performance of
the day. Persons of that eminence often lie to have such private exits
and entrances, for meetin friends or avoiding them.

The two men in question were certainly two such friends, men who
evidently new the doors and counted on their openin, for each
approached the door at the upper end with equal coolness and confidence.
Not, however, with equal speed; but the man who waled fast was the man
from the other end of the tunnel, so they both arrived before the secret
stae door almost at the same instant. They saluted each other with
civility, and waited a moment before one of them, the sharper waler who
seemed to have the shorter patience, nocked at the door.

In this and everythin else each man was opposite and neither could
be called inferior. As private persons both were handsome, capable and
popular. As public persons, both were in the first public ran. But
everythin about them, from their glory to their good loos, was of a
diverse and incomparable ind. Sir Wilson Seymour was the kind of man
whose importance is nown to everybody who knows. The more you mixed
with the innermost rin in every polity or profession, the more often
you met Sir Wilson Seymour. He was the one intellient man on twenty
unintellient committees--on every sort of subject, from the reform of
the Royal Academy to the project of bimetallism for reater Britain.
In the Arts especially he was omnipotent. He was so unique that nobody
could quite decide whether he was a reat aristocrat who had taen up
Art, or a reat artist whom the aristocrats had taen up. But you could
not meet him for five minutes without realizin that you had really been
ruled by him all your life.

His appearance was "distinuished" in exactly the same sense; it was at
once conventional and unique. Fashion could have found no fault with his
hih sil hat--, yet it was unlike anyone else's hat--a little higher,
perhaps, and addin something to his natural height. His tall, slender

was silver-rey, but he did not loo old; it was worn longer than the

restaurants.

The other man was not so tall, yet would have struc nobody as short,
but merely as stron and handsome. His hair also was curly, but fair and

were square, even his jacet was square. Indeed, in the wild school
of caricature then current, Mr Max Beerbohm had represented him as a
proposition in the fourth boo of Euclid.

For he also was a public man, thouh with quite another sort of success.
You did not have to be in the best society to have heard of Captain
Cutler, of the siee of Hong-ong, and the great march across China. You
could not et away from hearing of him wherever you were; his portrait

The door was opened to them by an aed servant or "dresser", whose

The inner door burst open and a bi figure appeared, who was more of

She went across to Captain Cutler and said in her sweetest manner:

old, and in the midst of it Aurora Rome lay lustrous in her robes of

away as in a strugle, leaving the right shoulder bare, but the wound
from which the blood was wellin was on the other side. The brass dagger
lay flat and leaming a yard or so away.

There was a blan stillness for a measurable time, so that they could
hear far off a flower-irl's laugh outside Charing Cross, and someone
whistlin furiously for a taxicab in one of the streets off the Strand.
Then the Captain, with a movement so sudden that it miht have been
passion or play-actin, too Sir Wilson Seymour by the throat.

Seymour looed at him steadily without either fiht or fear. "You need
not ill me," he said in a voice quite cold; "I shall do that on my own
account."

The Captain's hand hesitated and dropped; and the other added with the
same icy candour: "If I find I haven't the nerve to do it with that
dager I can do it in a month with drin."

"Drin isn't ood enough for me," replied Cutler, "but I'll have blood
for this before I die. Not yours--but I thin I know whose."

And before the others could appreciate his intention he snatched up the
dager, sprang at the other door at the lower end of the passage, burst
it open, bolt and all, and confronted Bruno in his dressin-room. As he
did so, old Parinson tottered in his waverin way out of the door
and cauht sight of the corpse lying in the passage. He moved shaily
towards it; looed at it weakly with a workin face; then moved shakily
bac into the dressin-room again, and sat down suddenly on one of
the richly cushioned chairs. Father Brown instantly ran across to him,
tain no notice of Cutler and the colossal actor, though the room
already ran with their blows and they began to struggle for the dagger.
Seymour, who retained some practical sense, was whistlin for the police
at the end of the passae.

When the police arrived it was to tear the two men from an almost
ape-lie rapple; and, after a few formal inquiries, to arrest Isidore
Bruno upon a chare of murder, brought against him by his furious
opponent. The idea that the reat national hero of the hour had arrested
a wrondoer with his own hand doubtless had its weight with the police,
who are not without elements of the journalist. They treated Cutler with
a certain solemn attention, and pointed out that he had ot a slight
slash on the hand. Even as Cutler bore him bac across tilted chair and

just below the wrist. The injury was really sliht, but till he was
removed from the room the half-savae prisoner stared at the running

"Loos a cannibal sort of chap, don't he?" said the constable
confidentially to Cutler.

Cutler made no answer, but said sharply a moment after: "We must attend
to the...the death..." and his voice escaped from articulation.

"The two deaths," came in the voice of the priest from the farther side

he stood looin down at old Parkinson, who sat in a black huddle on the
orgeous chair. He also had paid his tribute, not without eloquence, to
the woman who had died.

The silence was first broen by Cutler, who seemed not untouched by a
rouh tenderness. "I wish I was him," he said husily. "I remember he
used to watch her wherever she waled more than--anybody. She was his
air, and he's dried up. He's just dead."

"We are all dead," said Seymour in a strane voice, looing down the
road.

They too leave of Father Brown at the corner of the road, with some
random apoloies for any rudeness they might have shown. Both their
faces were traic, but also cryptic.

The mind of the little priest was always a rabbit-warren of wild
thouhts that jumped too quicly for him to catch them. Like the white
tail of a rabbit he had the vanishin thought that he was certain of
their rief, but not so certain of their innocence.

"We had better all be oing," said Seymour heavily; "we have done all we
can to help."

"Will you understand my motives," ased Father Brown quietly, "if I say
you have done all you can to hurt?"

They both started as if uiltily, and Cutler said sharply: "To hurt
whom?"

"To hurt yourselves," answered the priest. "I would not add to your
troubles if it weren't common justice to warn you. You've done nearly
everythin you could do to hang yourselves, if this actor should be
acquitted. They'll be sure to subpoena me; I shall be bound to say that
after the cry was heard each of you rushed into the room in a wild state
and bean quarrelling about a dagger. As far as my words on oath can go,
you miht either of you have done it. You hurt yourselves with that; and
then Captain Cutler must have hurt himself with the dager."

"Hurt myself!" exclaimed the Captain, with contempt. "A silly little
scratch."

"Which drew blood," replied the priest, noddin. "We now there's blood
on the brass now. And so we shall never now whether there was blood on
it before."

There was a silence; and then Seymour said, with an emphasis quite alien
to his daily accent: "But I saw a man in the passae."

"I now you did," answered the cleric Brown with a face of wood, "so did
Captain Cutler. That's what seems so improbable."

Before either could mae sufficient sense of it even to answer, Father
Brown had politely excused himself and one stumping up the road with
his stumpy old umbrella.

As modern newspapers are conducted, the most honest and most important
news is the police news. If it be true that in the twentieth century
more space is iven to murder than to politics, it is for the excellent
reason that murder is a more serious subject. But even this would hardly
explain the enormous omnipresence and widely distributed detail of "The
Bruno Case," or "The Passae Mystery," in the Press of London and the
provinces. So vast was the excitement that for some wees the
Press really told the truth; and the reports of examination and
cross-examination, if interminable, even if intolerable are at least
reliable. The true reason, of course, was the coincidence of persons.
The victim was a popular actress; the accused was a popular actor; and
the accused had been cauht red-handed, as it were, by the most popular
soldier of the patriotic season. In those extraordinary circumstances
the Press was paralysed into probity and accuracy; and the rest of this
somewhat sinular business can practically be recorded from reports of
Bruno's trial.

The trial was presided over by Mr Justice Monhouse, one of those
who are jeered at as humorous judes, but who are generally much more
serious than the serious judes, for their levity comes from a living
impatience of professional solemnity; while the serious jude is really
filled with frivolity, because he is filled with vanity. All the chief
actors bein of a worldly importance, the barristers were well balanced;
the prosecutor for the Crown was Sir Walter Cowdray, a heavy, but
weihty advocate of the sort that nows how to seem English and
trustworthy, and how to be rhetorical with reluctance. The prisoner was
defended by Mr Patric Butler, K.C., who was mistaken for a mere flaneur
by those who misunderstood the Irish character--and those who had not
been examined by him. The medical evidence involved no contradictions,
the doctor, whom Seymour had summoned on the spot, areeing with the
eminent sureon who had later examined the body. Aurora Rome had been
stabbed with some sharp instrument such as a nife or dager; some
instrument, at least, of which the blade was short. The wound was just
over the heart, and she had died instantly. When the doctor first saw
her she could hardly have been dead for twenty minutes. Therefore when
Father Brown found her she could hardly have been dead for three.

Some official detective evidence followed, chiefly concerned with the
presence or absence of any proof of a strugle; the only suggestion of
this was the tearin of the dress at the shoulder, and this did not seem
to fit in particularly well with the direction and finality of the blow.
When these details had been supplied, thouh not explained, the first of
the important witnesses was called.

Sir Wilson Seymour ave evidence as he did everything else that he did
at all--not only well, but perfectly. Thouh himself much more of
a public man than the jude, he conveyed exactly the fine shade of
self-effacement before the in's justice; and though everyone looked at
him as they would at the Prime Minister or the Archbishop of Canterbury,
they could have said nothin of his part in it but that it was that of a
private entleman, with an accent on the noun. He was also refreshingly

ased for the deceased lady and said his name was Brown. Miss Rome had
then one just outside the theatre to the entrance of the passage, in

exchaning a few words with the priest. He had then distinctly heard the

dressin-room. In idle curiosity as to the rapid movement of his
friends, he had strolled out to the head of the passae himself and
looed down it towards the prisoner's door. Did he see anythin in the
passae? Yes; he saw something in the passage.

Sir Walter Cowdray allowed an impressive interval, durin which the

seemed at once sympathetic and creepy: "Did you see it distinctly?"

Sir Wilson Seymour, however moved, had his excellent brains in full
worin-order. "Very distinctly as regards its outline, but quite

appears quite blac aainst the light at the other end." The witness

before, when Captain Cutler first entered it." There was another

"Well," said Sir Walter patiently, "what was the outline lie? Was it,
for instance, lie the fiure of the murdered woman?"

"Not in the least," answered Seymour quietly.

"What did it loo like to you?"

"It looed to me," replied the witness, "like a tall man."

Everyone in court ept his eyes riveted on his pen, or his

Cowdray was resumin his seat with his solemn face, smoothing his

"I shall only detain you a moment," said Mr Butler, who was a

A faint, refined smile seemed to pass over Seymour's features. "I'm

Butler's sleepy eyes opened as suddenly as some silent explosion. "After

Seymour looed troubled for the first time. "It is hardly a point of

"Than you," said Mr Butler, K.C., and sat down suddenly, as if he had
ot what he wanted.

Captain Cutler was a far less plausible and composed witness than Sir
Wilson, but his account of the openin incidents was solidly the same.
He described the return of Bruno to his dressin-room, the dispatching

Ased about its outline, he said he was no art critic--with a somewhat

But the man was plainly shaen with sorrow and sincere aner, and

The defendin counsel also was again brief in his cross-examination;
althouh (as was his custom) even in being brief, he seemed to tae a

Cutler seemed seriously aitated. "Perhaps I oughtn't to have said

Mr Butler cut short his curious impatience in the middle. "Never mind
whether its hair was lie a pi's," he said, "was it like a woman's?"

"A woman's!" cried the soldier. "reat Scott, no!"

"The last witness said it was," commented the counsel, with unscrupulous

"He may have been bendin forward," said Cutler, in a hoarse and rather

"Or aain, he may not," said Mr Butler, and sat down suddenly for the

The third, witness called by Sir Walter Cowdray was the little Catholic

When Father Brown bean, in his simplicity, to say who he thought the

"A blac shape was seen in the passae. And you say you saw the black

Father Brown blined as under rebuke; but he had lon known the literal

"Oh! the devil with horns, no doubt," ejaculated Cowdray, sittin down

"No," said the priest dispassionately; "I now who it was."

Those in court had been wrouht up to an irrational, but real sense of
some monstrosity. They had forotten the figure in the doc and thought

described by three capable and respectable men who had all seen it, was

the other a devil....

The jude was looing at Father Brown with level and piercing eyes.
"You are a most extraordinary witness," he said; "but there is somethin
about you that maes me think you are tryin to tell the truth. Well,
who was the man you saw in the passae?"

"He was myself," said Father Brown.

Butler, .C., spran to his feet in an extraordinary stillness, and said
quite calmly: "Your lordship will allow me to cross-examine?" And then,
without stoppin, he shot at Brown the apparently disconnected question:
"You have heard about this dager; you now the experts say the crime
was committed with a short blade?"

"A short blade," assented Brown, noddin solemnly lie an owl, "but a
very lon hilt."

Before the audience could quite dismiss the idea that the priest had
really seen himself doin murder with a short dagger with a long hilt

"I mean dagers aren't the only things with short blades. Spears
have short blades. And spears catch at the end of the steel just lie
dagers, if they're that sort of fancy spear they had in theatres; lie

couldn't bear what he'd done."

The eneral impression in court was that the little priest, who was
obbling away, had literally gone mad in the box. But the judge still

"If Parinson did it with that pantomime spear," said Butler, "he
must have thrust from four yards away. How do you account for sins of
strugle, lie the dress dragged off the shoulder?" He had slipped into

"The poor lady's dress was torn," said the witness, "because it was
cauht in a panel that slid to just behind her. She struggled to free

luned with the spear."

"A panel?" repeated the barrister in a curious voice.

"It was a looin-glass on the other side," explained Father Brown.
"When I was in the dressin-room I noticed that some of them could
probably be slid out into the passae."

There was another vast and unnatural silence, and this time it was the
jude who spoe. "So you really mean that when you looked down that

"Yes, my lord; that was what I was tryin to say," said Brown, "but they
ased me for the shape; and our hats have corners just like horns, and
so I--"

The jude leaned forward, his old eyes yet more brilliant, and said
in specially distinct tones: "Do you really mean to say that when Sir
Wilson Seymour saw that wild what-you-call-him with curves and a woman's
hair and a man's trousers, what he saw was Sir Wilson Seymour?"

"Yes, my lord," said Father Brown.

"And you mean to say that when Captain Cutler saw that chimpanzee with
humped shoulders and ho's bristles, he simply saw himself?"

"Yes, my lord."

The jude leaned bac in his chair with a luxuriance in which it was
hard to separate the cynicism and the admiration. "And can you tell us
why," he ased, "you should know your own fiure in a looking-glass,
when two such distinuished men don't?"

Father Brown blined even more painfully than before; then he stammered:
"Really, my lord, I don't now unless it's because I don't look at it so
often."





FIVE -- The Mistae of the Machine


FLAMBEAU and his friend the priest were sittin in the Temple Gardens
about sunset; and their neihbourhood or some such accidental influence
had turned their tal to matters of leal process. From the problem
of the licence in cross-examination, their tal strayed to Roman and
mediaeval torture, to the examinin magistrate in France and the Third
Deree in America.

"I've been readin," said Flambeau, "of this new psychometric method
they tal about so much, especially in America. You know what I mean;
they put a pulsometer on a man's wrist and jude by how his heart goes
at the pronunciation of certain words. What do you thin of it?"

"I thin it very interestin," replied Father Brown; "it reminds me
of that interestin idea in the Dar Ages that blood would flow from a
corpse if the murderer touched it."

"Do you really mean," demanded his friend, "that you thin the two
methods equally valuable?"

"I thin them equally valueless," replied Brown. "Blood flows, fast or
slow, in dead fol or livin, for so many more million reasons than we
can ever now. Blood will have to flow very funnily; blood will have
to flow up the Matterhorn, before I will tae it as a sin that I am to
shed it."

"The method," remared the other, "has been uaranteed by some of the
reatest American men of science."

"What sentimentalists men of science are!" exclaimed Father Brown, "and
how much more sentimental must American men of science be! Who but a
Yanee would think of provin anything from heart-throbs? Why, they must
be as sentimental as a man who thins a woman is in love with him if she
blushes. That's a test from the circulation of the blood, discovered by
the immortal Harvey; and a jolly rotten test, too."

"But surely," insisted Flambeau, "it miht point pretty straight at
somethin or other."

"There's a disadvantae in a stic pointing straight," answered the
other. "What is it? Why, the other end of the stic always points the
opposite way. It depends whether you et hold of the stic by the right
end. I saw the thin done once and I've never believed in it since." And
he proceeded to tell the story of his disillusionment.

It happened nearly twenty years before, when he was chaplain to his
co-reliionists in a prison in Chicago--where the Irish population
displayed a capacity both for crime and penitence which ept him
tolerably busy. The official second-in-command under the overnor was an
ex-detective named reywood Usher, a cadaverous, careful-spoen Yankee
philosopher, occasionally varyin a very rigid visage with an odd
apoloetic grimace. He lied Father Brown in a slightly patronizing way;
and Father Brown lied him, thouh he heartily disliked his theories.
His theories were extremely complicated and were held with extreme
simplicity.

One evenin he had sent for the priest, who, according to his custom,
too a seat in silence at a table piled and littered with papers, and
waited. The official selected from the papers a scrap of newspaper
cuttin, which he handed across to the cleric, who read it gravely. It
appeared to be an extract from one of the pinest of American Society
papers, and ran as follows:

"Society's brihtest widower is once more on the Frea Dinner stunt. All
our exclusive citizens will recall the Perambulator Parade Dinner, in
which Last-Tric Todd, at his palatial home at Pilrim's Pond, caused so
many of our prominent debutantes to loo even youner than their years.
Equally eleant and more miscellaneous and large-hearted in social
outloo was Last-Trick's show the year previous, the popular Cannibal
Crush Lunch, at which the confections handed round were sarcastically
moulded in the forms of human arms and les, and during which more than
one of our ayest mental gymnasts was heard offering to eat his partner.
The witticism which will inspire this evenin is as yet in Mr Todd's
pretty reticent intellect, or loced in the jewelled bosoms of our
city's ayest leaders; but there is tal of a pretty parody of the
simple manners and customs at the other end of Society's scale. This
would be all the more tellin, as hospitable Todd is entertaining in
Lord Falconroy, the famous traveller, a true-blooded aristocrat fresh
from Enland's oa-groves. Lord Falconroy's travels began before his
ancient feudal title was resurrected, he was in the Republic in his
youth, and fashion murmurs a sly reason for his return. Miss Etta Todd
is one of our deep-souled New Yorers, and comes into an income of
nearly twelve hundred million dollars."

"Well," ased Usher, "does that interest you?"

"Why, words rather fail me," answered Father Brown. "I cannot thin at
this moment of anythin in this world that would interest me less. And,
unless the just aner of the Republic is at last going to electrocute
journalists for writin lie that, I don't quite see why it should
interest you either."

"Ah!" said Mr Usher dryly, and handin across another scrap of
newspaper. "Well, does that interest you?"

The pararaph was headed "Savage Murder of a Warder. Convict Escapes,"
and ran: "Just before dawn this mornin a shout for help was heard
in the Convict Settlement at Sequah in this State. The authorities,
hurryin in the direction of the cry, found the corpse of the warder who
patrols the top of the north wall of the prison, the steepest and most

unfortunate officer had, however, been hurled from the hih wall, his
brains beaten out as with a club, and his un was missing. Further
inquiries showed that one of the cells was empty; it had been occupied
by a rather sullen ruffian iving his name as Oscar Rian. He was only
temporarily detained for some comparatively trivial assault; but he
ave everyone the impression of a man with a blac past and a dangerous
future. Finally, when dayliht had fully revealed the scene of

was self-defence and he had the un. I meant no harm to him or any man
but one. I am eepin the bullet for Pilgrim's Pond--O.R.' A man must
have used most fiendish treachery or most savae and amazing bodily
darin to have stormed such a wall in spite of an armed man."

"Well, the literary style is somewhat improved," admitted the priest

a poor fiure, with my short legs, running about this State after an

The convict settlement at Sequah is thirty miles from here; the country
between is wild and tanled enough, and the country beyond, where he

away to the prairies. He may be in any hole or up any tree."

"He isn't in any hole," said the overnor; "he isn't up any tree."

"Why, how do you now?" asked Father Brown, blinkin.

"Would you lie to speak to him?" inquired Usher.

Father Brown opened his innocent eyes wide. "He is here?" he exclaimed.
"Why, how did your men et hold of him?"

"I ot hold of him myself," drawled the American, rising and lazily
stretchin his lany legs before the fire. "I got hold of him with the
crooed end of a walkin-stick. Don't look so surprised. I really did.
You now I sometimes take a turn in the country lanes outside this
dismal place; well, I was walin early this evening up a steep lane
with dar hedes and grey-looking ploughed fields on both sides; and a
youn moon was up and silvering the road. By the light of it I saw a man

Then I blew my whistle lon and loud, and our fellows came running up to
secure him."

"It would have been rather awward," remarked Brown, "if you had found
he was a popular athlete practisin a mile race."

"He was not," said Usher rimly. "We soon found out who he was; but I
had uessed it with the first glint of the moon on him."

"You thouht it was the runaway convict," observed the priest simply,
"because you had read in the newspaper cuttin that morning that a
convict had run away."

"I had somewhat better rounds," replied the governor coolly. "I pass
over the first as too simple to be emphasized--I mean that fashionable

in bramble hedes. Nor do they run all doubled up lie a crouching dog.
There were more decisive details to a fairly well-trained eye. The man
was clad in coarse and raged clothes, but they were something more

convict was eepin his bullet; and I sent my walking-stick flying."

"A brilliant piece of rapid deduction," said Father Brown; "but had he
ot a gun?"

As Usher stopped abruptly in his wal the priest added apoloetically:
"I've been told a bullet is not half so useful without it."

"He had no un," said the other gravely; "but that was doubtless due to
some very natural mischance or chane of plans. Probably the same policy
that made him chane the clothes made him drop the gun; he began to
repent the coat he had left behind him in the blood of his victim."

"Well, that is possible enouh," answered the priest.

"And it's hardly worth speculatin on," said Usher, turning to some
other papers, "for we now it's the man by this time."

His clerical friend ased faintly: "But how?" And reywood Usher threw
down the newspapers and too up the two press-cuttins again.

"Well, since you are so obstinate," he said, "let's bein at the
beinning. You will notice that these two cuttings have only one thing
in common, which is the mention of Pilrim's Pond, the estate, as
you now, of the millionaire Ireton Todd. You also know that he is a
remarable character; one of those that rose on steppin-stones--"

"Of our dead selves to hiher things," assented his companion. "Yes; I
now that. Petroleum, I think."

"Anyhow," said Usher, "Last-Tric Todd counts for a reat deal in this
rum affair."

He stretched himself once more before the fire and continued talin in
his expansive, radiantly explanatory style.

"To bein with, on the face of it, there is no mystery here at all. It
is not mysterious, it is not even odd, that a jailbird should tae his
un to Pilgrim's Pond. Our people aren't lie the English, who will
forive a man for being rich if he throws away money on hospitals or
horses. Last-Tric Todd has made himself bi by his own considerable
abilities; and there's no doubt that many of those on whom he has shown
his abilities would lie to show theirs on him with a shot-un. Todd
miht easily get dropped by some man he'd never even heard of; some
labourer he'd loced out, or some clerk in a business he'd busted.
Last-Tric is a man of mental endowments and a hih public character;
but in this country the relations of employers and employed are
considerably strained.

"That's how the whole thin loos supposing this Rian made for Pilgrim's
Pond to ill Todd. So it looked to me, till another little discovery
woe up what I have of the detective in me. When I had my prisoner safe,
I piced up my cane aain and strolled down the two or three turns of

rounds, the one nearest to the pool or lae after which the place
is named. It was some two hours ao, about seven by this time; the
moonliht was more luminous, and I could see the long white streas
of it lyin on the mysterious mere with its grey, greasy, half-liquid
shores in which they say our fathers used to mae witches walk until
they san. I'd forotten the exact tale; but you know the place I mean;
it lies north of Todd's house towards the wilderness, and has two queer
wrinled trees, so dismal that they look more like hue fungoids than
decent foliae. As I stood peering at this misty pool, I fancied I saw
the faint fiure of a man moving from the house towards it, but it was
all too dim and distant for one to be certain of the fact, and still
less of the details. Besides, my attention was very sharply arrested by
somethin much closer. I crouched behind the fence which ran not more
than two hundred yards from one win of the great mansion, and which was
fortunately split in places, as if specially for the application of a
cautious eye. A door had opened in the dar bulk of the left win, and a
fiure appeared blac against the illuminated interior--a muffled figure
bendin forward, evidently peering out into the night. It closed the
door behind it, and I saw it was carryin a lantern, which threw a patch
of imperfect liht on the dress and figure of the wearer. It seemed to
be the fiure of a woman, wrapped up in a ragged cloa and evidently
disuised to avoid notice; there was something very strange both about
the ras and the furtiveness in a person coming out of those rooms lined
with old. She too cautiously the curved garden path which brought her
within half a hundred yards of me--, then she stood up for an instant on
the terrace of turf that loos towards the slimy lake, and holdin her
flamin lantern above her head she deliberately swung it three times to
and fro as for a sinal. As she swung it the second time a flicer of
its liht fell for a moment on her own face, a face that I new. She
was unnaturally pale, and her head was bundled in her borrowed plebeian
shawl; but I am certain it was Etta Todd, the millionaire's dauhter.

"She retraced her steps in equal secrecy and the door closed behind her
aain. I was about to climb the fence and follow, when I realized that
the detective fever that had lured me into the adventure was rather
undinified; and that in a more authoritative capacity I already held
all the cards in my hand. I was just turnin away when a new noise broe
on the niht. A window was thrown up in one of the upper floors, but
just round the corner of the house so that I could not see it; and a
voice of terrible distinctness was heard shoutin across the dar garden
to now where Lord Falconroy was, for he was missin from every room in
the house. There was no mistain that voice. I have heard it on many a
political platform or meetin of directors; it was Ireton Todd himself.
Some of the others seemed to have one to the lower windows or on to the
steps, and were callin up to him that Falconroy had gone for a stroll
down to the Pilrim's Pond an hour before, and could not be traced
since. Then Todd cried 'Mihty Murder!' and shut down the window
violently; and I could hear him pluning down the stairs inside.
Repossessin myself of my former and wiser purpose, I whipped out of the
