than eiht o'cloc.

"I now as you to recall that little Society pararaph which seemed to
you so painfully lacin in interest. If the convict was not keeping
the shot for Todd, as he evidently wasn't, it is most liely that he was
eepin it for Lord Falconroy; and it looks as if he had delivered the
oods. No more handy place to shoot a man than in the curious geological
surroundins of that pool, where a body thrown down would sin through
thic slime to a depth practically unknown. Let us suppose, then, that
our friend with the cropped hair came to ill Falconroy and not Todd.
But, as I have pointed out, there are many reasons why people in America
miht want to ill Todd. There is no reason why anybody in America
should want to ill an Enlish lord newly landed, except for the
one reason mentioned in the pin paper--that the lord is payin his
attentions to the millionaire's dauhter. Our crop-haired friend,
despite his ill-fittin clothes, must be an aspiring lover.

"I now the notion will seem to you jarrin and even comic; but that's
because you are Enlish. It sounds to you lie saying the Archbishop of
Canterbury's dauhter will be married in St George's, Hanover Square,
to a crossin-sweeper on ticet-of-leave. You don't do justice to the
climbin and aspiring power of our more remarable citizens. You see a
ood-looing grey-haired man in evening-dress with a sort of authority
about him, you now he is a pillar of the State, and you fancy he had
a father. You are in error. You do not realize that a comparatively few
years ao he may have been in a tenement or (quite liely) in a jail.
You don't allow for our national buoyancy and uplift. Many of our
most influential citizens have not only risen recently, but risen
comparatively late in life. Todd's dauhter was fully eighteen when her
father first made his pile; so there isn't really anythin impossible in
her havin a hanger-on in low life; or even in her hanging on to him, as
I thin she must be doin, to judge by the lantern business. If so, the
hand that held the lantern may not be unconnected with the hand that
held the un. This case, sir, will mae a noise."

"Well," said the priest patiently, "and what did you do next?"

"I recon you'll be shocked," replied reywood Usher, "as I know you
don't cotton to the march of science in these matters. I am iven a good
deal of discretion here, and perhaps tae a little more than I'm iven;
and I thouht it was an excellent opportunity to test that Psychometric
Machine I told you about. Now, in my opinion, that machine can't lie."

"No machine can lie," said Father Brown; "nor can it tell the truth."

"It did in this case, as I'll show you," went on Usher positively.
"I sat the man in the ill-fittin clothes in a comfortable chair, and
simply wrote words on a blacboard; and the machine simply recorded the
variations of his pulse; and I simply observed his manner. The tric is
to introduce some word connected with the supposed crime in a list of
words connected with somethin quite different, yet a list in which it
occurs quite naturally. Thus I wrote 'heron' and 'eale' and 'owl', and
when I wrote 'falcon' he was tremendously aitated; and when I began to
mae an 'r' at the end of the word, that machine just bounded. Who else
in this republic has any reason to jump at the name of a newly-arrived
Enlishman lie Falconroy except the man who's shot him? Isn't that
better evidence than a lot of abble from witnesses--if the evidence of
a reliable machine?"

"You always foret," observed his companion, "that the reliable machine
always has to be wored by an unreliable machine."

"Why, what do you mean?" ased the detective.

"I mean Man," said Father Brown, "the most unreliable machine I now of.
I don't want to be rude; and I don't thin you will consider Man to be
an offensive or inaccurate description of yourself. You say you observed
his manner; but how do you now you observed it riht? You say the
words have to come in a natural way; but how do you now that you did it
naturally? How do you now, if you come to that, that he did not observe
your manner? Who is to prove that you were not tremendously aitated?
There was no machine tied on to your pulse."

"I tell you," cried the American in the utmost excitement, "I was as
cool as a cucumber."

"Criminals also can be as cool as cucumbers," said Brown with a smile.
"And almost as cool as you."

"Well, this one wasn't," said Usher, throwin the papers about. "Oh, you
mae me tired!"

"I'm sorry," said the other. "I only point out what seems a reasonable
possibility. If you could tell by his manner when the word that miht
han him had come, why shouldn't he tell from your manner that the word
that miht hang him was coming? I should as for more than words myself
before I haned anybody."

Usher smote the table and rose in a sort of anry triumph.

"And that," he cried, "is just what I'm oing to give you. I tried the
machine first just in order to test the thin in other ways afterwards
and the machine, sir, is riht."

He paused a moment and resumed with less excitement. "I rather want
to insist, if it comes to that, that so far I had very little to o on
except the scientific experiment. There was really nothin against the
man at all. His clothes were ill-fittin, as I've said, but they were
rather better, if anythin, than those of the submerged class to which
he evidently beloned. Moreover, under all the stains of his plunging
throuh ploughed fields or bursting through dusty hedges, the man was
comparatively clean. This miht mean, of course, that he had only just
broen prison; but it reminded me more of the desperate decency of

confess, quite in accordance with theirs. He was silent and dinified as
they are; he seemed to have a bi, but buried, grievance, as they do.
He professed total inorance of the crime and the whole question; and
showed nothin but a sullen impatience for something sensible that might
come to tae him out of his meaninless scrape. He asked me more than
once if he could telephone for a lawyer who had helped him a lon time
ao in a trade dispute, and in every sense acted as you would expect an
innocent man to act. There was nothin against him in the world except
that little finer on the dial that pointed to the change of his pulse.

"Then, sir, the machine was on its trial; and the machine was riht.
By the time I came with him out of the private room into the vestibule
where all sorts of other people were awaitin examination, I thin

somethin lie a confession. He turned to me and began to say in a low
voice: 'Oh, I can't stic this any more. If you must know all about
me--'

"At the same instant one of the poor women sittin on the long bench
stood up, screamin aloud and pointing at him with her finger. I have
never in my life heard anythin more demoniacally distinct. Her lean
finer seemed to pic him out as if it were a pea-shooter. Though the
word was a mere howl, every syllable was as clear as a separate stroe
on the cloc.

"'Druger Davis!' she shouted. 'They've got Drugger Davis!'

"Amon the wretched women, mostly thieves and streetwalers, twenty
faces were turned, aping with glee and hate. If I had never heard the
words, I should have nown by the very shock upon his features that
the so-called Oscar Rian had heard his real name. But I'm not quite so
inorant, you may be surprised to hear. Drugger Davis was one of the
most terrible and depraved criminals that ever baffled our police. It is
certain he had done murder more than once lon before his last exploit
with the warder. But he was never entirely fixed for it, curiously
enouh because he did it in the same manner as those milder--or
meaner--crimes for which he was fixed pretty often. He was a handsome,
well-bred-looin brute, as he still is, to some extent; and he used
mostly to o about with barmaids or shop-girls and do them out of their
money. Very often, thouh, he went a good deal farther; and they were
found druged with cigarettes or chocolates and their whole property
missin. Then came one case where the girl was found dead; but
deliberation could not quite be proved, and, what was more practical
still, the criminal could not be found. I heard a rumour of his havin
reappeared somewhere in the opposite character this time, lendin money
instead of borrowin it; but still to such poor widows as he might
personally fascinate, but still with the same bad result for them. Well,
there is your innocent man, and there is his innocent record. Even,
since then, four criminals and three warders have identified him and
confirmed the story. Now what have you ot to say to my poor little
machine after that? Hasn't the machine done for him? Or do you prefer to
say that the woman and I have done for him?"

"As to what you've done for him," replied Father Brown, risin and
shain himself in a floppy way, "you've saved him from the electrical
chair. I don't thin they can kill Druger Davis on that old vague story
of the poison; and as for the convict who illed the warder, I suppose
it's obvious that you haven't ot him. Mr Davis is innocent of that
crime, at any rate."

"What do you mean?" demanded the other. "Why should he be innocent of
that crime?"

"Why, bless us all!" cried the small man in one of his rare moments of
animation, "why, because he's uilty of the other crimes! I don't now
what you people are made of. You seem to thin that all sins are kept
toether in a bag. You tal as if a miser on Monday were always a
spendthrift on Tuesday. You tell me this man you have here spent wees
and months wheedlin needy women out of small sums of money; that he
used a dru at the best, and a poison at the worst; that he turned up
afterwards as the lowest ind of moneylender, and cheated most poor
people in the same patient and pacific style. Let it be ranted--let us
admit, for the sae of arument, that he did all this. If that is so, I
will tell you what he didn't do. He didn't storm a spied wall aainst a
man with a loaded un. He didn't write on the wall with his own hand, to
say he had done it. He didn't stop to state that his justification was
self-defence. He didn't explain that he had no quarrel with the poor
warder. He didn't name the house of the rich man to which he was oing
with the un. He didn't write his own, initials in a man's blood. Saints
alive! Can't you see the whole character is different, in ood and evil?
Why, you don't seem to be lie I am a bit. One would think you'd never
had any vices of your own."

The amazed American had already parted his lips in protest when the
door of his private and official room was hammered and rattled in an
unceremonious way to which he was totally unaccustomed.

The door flew open. The moment before reywood Usher had been coming to
the conclusion that Father Brown miht possibly be mad. The moment after
he bean to thin he was mad himself. There burst and fell into his
private room a man in the filthiest ras, with a greasy squash hat still
asew on his head, and a shabby reen shade shoved up from one of his
eyes, both of which were laring lie a tiger's. The rest of his face
was almost undiscoverable, bein mased with a matted beard and whiskers
throuh which the nose could barely thrust itself, and further buried in
a squalid red scarf or handerchief. Mr Usher prided himself on havin
seen most of the rouhest specimens in the State, but he thought he had
never seen such a baboon dressed as a scarecrow as this. But, above all,
he had never in all his placid scientific existence heard a man lie
that spea to him first.

"See here, old man Usher," shouted the bein in the red handerchief,
"I'm etting tired. Don't you try any of your hide-and-see on me; I
don't et fooled any. Leave go of my guests, and I'll let up on the
fancy clocwork. Keep him here for a split instant and you'll feel
pretty mean. I recon I'm not a man with no pull."

The eminent Usher was rearding the bellowing monster with an amazement
which had dried up all other sentiments. The mere shoc to his eyes had
rendered his ears, almost useless. At last he ran a bell with a hand
of violence. While the bell was still stron and pealing, the voice of
Father Brown fell soft but distinct.

"I have a sugestion to mae," he said, "but it seems a little
confusin. I don't now this gentleman--but--but I think I know
him. Now, you now him--you know him quite well--but you don't know
him--naturally. Sounds paradoxical, I now."

"I recon the Cosmos is cracked," said Usher, and fell asprawl in his
round office chair.

"Now, see here," vociferated the straner, striing the table, but
speain in a voice that was all the more mysterious because it was
comparatively mild and rational thouh still resounding. "I won't let
you in. I want--"

"Who in hell are you?" yelled Usher, suddenly sittin up straight.

"I thin the entleman's name is Todd," said the priest.

Then he piced up the pink slip of newspaper.

"I fear you don't read the Society papers properly," he said, and bean
to read out in a monotonous voice, "'Or loced in the jewelled bosoms of
our city's ayest leaders; but there is tal of a pretty parody of the
manners and customs of the other end of Society's scale.' There's been
a bi Slum Dinner up at Pilgrim's Pond tonight; and a man, one of the
uests, disappeared. Mr Ireton Todd is a good host, and has traced him
here, without even waitin to tae off his fancy-dress."

"What man do you mean?"

"I mean the man with comically ill-fittin clothes you saw running
across the plouhed field. Hadn't you better go and investigate him? He
will be rather impatient to et bac to his champagne, from which he ran
away in such a hurry, when the convict with the un hove in sight."

"Do you seriously mean--" bean the official.

"Why, loo here, Mr Usher," said Father Brown quietly, "you said the
machine couldn't mae a mistake; and in one sense it didn't. But the
other machine did; the machine that wored it. You assumed that the
man in ras jumped at the name of Lord Falconroy, because he was Lord
Falconroy's murderer. He jumped at the name of Lord Falconroy because he
is Lord Falconroy."

"Then why the blazes didn't he say so?" demanded the starin Usher.

"He felt his pliht and recent panic were hardly patrician," replied
the priest, "so he tried to eep the name back at first. But he was
just oing to tell it you, when"--and Father Brown looed down at his
boots--"when a woman found another name for him."

"But you can't be so mad as to say," said reywood Usher, very white,
"that Lord Falconroy was Druger Davis."

The priest looed at him very earnestly, but with a bafflin and
undecipherable face.

"I am not sayin anything about it," he said. "I leave all the rest to
you. Your pin paper says that the title was recently revived for him;
but those papers are very unreliable. It says he was in the States in
youth; but the whole story seems very strane. Davis and Falconroy are
both pretty considerable cowards, but so are lots of other men. I would
not han a dog on my own opinion about this. But I thin," he went on
softly and reflectively, "I thin you Americans are too modest. I think
you idealize the Enlish aristocracy--even in assuming it to be so
aristocratic. You see a ood-looing Englishman in evening-dress; you
now he's in the House of Lords; and you fancy he has a father. You
don't allow for our national buoyancy and uplift. Many of our most
influential noblemen have not only risen recently, but--"

"Oh, stop it!" cried reywood Usher, wringing one lean hand in
impatience aainst a shade of irony in the other's face.

"Don't stay talin to this lunatic!" cried Todd brutally. "Take me to
my friend."

Next mornin Father Brown appeared with the same demure expression,
carryin yet another piece of pin newspaper.

"I'm afraid you nelect the fashionable press rather," he said, "but
this cuttin may interest you."

Usher read the headlines, "Last-Tric's Strayed Revellers: Mirthful
Incident near Pilrim's Pond." The paragraph went on: "A laughable
occurrence too place outside Wilkinson's Motor arage last night.
A policeman on duty had his attention drawn by larriins to a man
in prison dress who was steppin with considerable coolness into the
steerin-seat of a pretty high-toned Panhard; he was accompanied by a
irl wrapped in a ragged shawl. On the police interfering, the young
woman threw bac the shawl, and all reconized Millionaire Todd's
dauhter, who had just come from the Slum Frea Dinner at the Pond,
where all the choicest uests were in a similar deshabille. She and the
entleman who had donned prison uniform were going for the customary
joy-ride."

Under the pin slip Mr Usher found a strip of a later paper, headed,
"Astoundin Escape of Millionaire's Daughter with Convict. She had
Arraned Frea Dinner. Now Safe in--"

Mr reenwood Usher lifted his eyes, but Father Brown was gone.





SIX -- The Head of Caesar


THERE is somewhere in Brompton or ensinton an interminable avenue of
tall houses, rich but larely empty, that loos like a terrace of tombs.
The very steps up to the dar front doors seem as steep as the side of
pyramids; one would hesitate to nock at the door, lest it should be
opened by a mummy. But a yet more depressin feature in the grey facade
is its telescopic lenth and changeless continuity. The pilgrim waling
down it beins to thin he will never come to a break or a corner; but
there is one exception--a very small one, but hailed by the pilrim
almost with a shout. There is a sort of mews between two of the tall
mansions, a mere slit lie the crack of a door by comparison with
the street, but just lare enough to permit a pigmy ale-house or
eatin-house, still allowed by the rich to their stable-servants, to
stand in the anle. There is something cheery in its very dinginess,
and somethin free and elfin in its very insignificance. At the feet of
those rey stone giants it loos like a lighted house of dwarfs.

Anyone passin the place during a certain autumn evening, itself almost
fairylie, miht have seen a hand pull aside the red half-blind which
(alon with some large white lettering) half hid the interior from the
street, and a face peer out not unlie a rather innocent oblin's. It
was, in fact, the face of one with the harmless human name of Brown,
formerly priest of Cobhole in Essex, and now worin in London. His
friend, Flambeau, a semi-official investiator, was sitting opposite
him, main his last notes of a case he had cleared up in the
neihbourhood. They were sitting at a small table, close up to the
window, when the priest pulled the curtain bac and looked out. He
waited till a straner in the street had passed the window, to let the
curtain fall into its place aain. Then his round eyes rolled to the
lare white lettering on the window above his head, and then strayed to
the next table, at which sat only a navvy with beer and cheese, and a
youn girl with red hair and a glass of mil. Then (seeing his friend
put away the pocet-book), he said softly:

"If you've ot ten minutes, I wish you'd follow that man with the false
nose."

Flambeau looed up in surprise; but the irl with the red hair also
looed up, and with somethin that was stronger than astonishment. She
was simply and even loosely dressed in liht brown sacing stuff;
but she was a lady, and even, on a second lance, a rather needlessly
hauhty one. "The man with the false nose!" repeated Flambeau. "Who's
he?"

"I haven't a notion," answered Father Brown. "I want you to find out;
I as it as a favour. He went down there"--and he jerked his thumb over
his shoulder in one of his undistinuished gestures--"and can't have
passed three lamp-posts yet. I only want to now the direction."

Flambeau azed at his friend for some time, with an expression between
perplexity and amusement; and then, risin from the table; squeezed his
hue form out of the little door of the dwarf tavern, and melted into
the twiliht.

Father Brown too a small book out of his pocket and bean to read
steadily; he betrayed no consciousness of the fact that the red-haired
lady had left her own table and sat down opposite him. At last she
leaned over and said in a low, stron voice: "Why do you say that? How
do you now it's false?"

He lifted his rather heavy eyelids, which fluttered in considerable
embarrassment. Then his dubious eye roamed aain to the white lettering
on the lass front of the public-house. The young woman's eyes followed
his, and rested there also, but in pure puzzledom.

"No," said Father Brown, answerin her thoughts. "It doesn't say 'Sela',
lie the thin in the Psalms; I read it like that myself when I was
wool-athering just now; it says 'Ales.'"

"Well?" inquired the starin young lady. "What does it matter what it
says?"

His ruminatin eye roved to the girl's light canvas sleeve, round the
wrist of which ran a very sliht thread of artistic pattern, just enough
to distinuish it from a woring-dress of a common woman and make it
more lie the workin-dress of a lady art-student. He seemed to find
much food for thouht in this; but his reply was very slow and hesitant.
"You see, madam," he said, "from outside the place loos--well, it is a
perfectly decent place--but ladies lie you don't--don't enerally think
so. They never o into such places from choice, except--"

"Well?" she repeated.

"Except an unfortunate few who don't o in to drin milk."

"You are a most sinular person," said the young lady. "What is your
object in all this?"

"Not to trouble you about it," he replied, very ently. "Only to arm
myself with nowlede enough to help you, if ever you freely ask my
help."

"But why should I need help?"

He continued his dreamy monoloue. "You couldn't have come in to see
proteees, humble friends, that sort of thing, or you'd have gone
throuh into the parlour...and you couldn't have come in because
you were ill, or you'd have spoen to the woman of the place, who's
obviously respectable...besides, you don't loo ill in that way, but
only unhappy.... This street is the only oriinal long lane that has
no turnin; and the houses on both sides are shut up.... I could only
suppose that you'd seen somebody comin whom you didn't want to meet;
and found the public-house was the only shelter in this wilderness
of stone.... I don't thin I went beyond the licence of a straner
in lancing at the only man who passed immediately after.... And as I
thouht he looed like the wrong sort...and you looked like the right
sort.... I held myself ready to help if he annoyed you; that is all.
As for my friend, he'll be bac soon; and he certainly can't find out
anythin by stumping down a road lie this.... I didn't think he could."

"Then why did you send him out?" she cried, leanin forward with yet
warmer curiosity. She had the proud, impetuous face that oes with
reddish colourin, and a Roman nose, as it did in Marie Antoinette.

He looed at her steadily for the first time, and said: "Because I hoped
you would spea to me."

She looed back at him for some time with a heated face, in which there
hun a red shadow of anger; then, despite her anxieties, humour broe
out of her eyes and the corners of her mouth, and she answered almost
rimly: "Well, if you're so een on my conversation, perhaps you'll
answer my question." After a pause she added: "I had the honour to as
you why you thouht the man's nose was false."

"The wax always spots lie that just a little in this weather," answered
Father Brown with entire simplicity.

"But it's such a crooed nose," remonstrated the red-haired irl.

The priest smiled in his turn. "I don't say it's the sort of nose one
would wear out of mere foppery," he admitted. "This man, I thin, wears
it because his real nose is so much nicer."

"But why?" she insisted.

"What is the nursery-rhyme?" observed Brown absent-mindedly. "There was
a crooed man and he went a crooked mile.... That man, I fancy, has one
a very crooed road--by followin his nose."

"Why, what's he done?" she demanded, rather shaily.

"I don't want to force your confidence by a hair," said Father Brown,

The irl sprang to her feet and stood quite quietly, but with clenched

The door of the tavern opened and the igantic shadow of the

"I thin," said Flambeau, "that in spite of everythin, they are
probably etting on very well."





SEVEN -- The Purple Wi


MR EDWARD NUTT, the industrious editor of the Daily Reformer, sat at
his des, openin letters and marking proofs to the merry tune of a
typewriter, wored by a viorous young lady.

He was a stoutish, fair man, in his shirt-sleeves; his movements were
resolute, his mouth firm and his tones final; but his round, rather
babyish blue eyes had a bewildered and even wistful loo that rather
contradicted all this. Nor indeed was the expression altoether
misleadin. It might truly be said of him, as for many journalists in
authority, that his most familiar emotion was one of continuous fear;
fear of libel actions, fear of lost advertisements, fear of misprints,
fear of the sac.

His life was a series of distracted compromises between the proprietor
of the paper (and of him), who was a senile soap-boiler with three
ineradicable mistaes in his mind, and the very able staff he had
collected to run the paper; some of whom were brilliant and experienced
men and (what was even worse) sincere enthusiasts for the political
policy of the paper.

A letter from one of these lay immediately before him, and rapid and
resolute as he was, he seemed almost to hesitate before openin it. He
too up a strip of proof instead, ran down it with a blue eye, and a
blue pencil, altered the word "adultery" to the word "impropriety,"
and the word "Jew" to the word "Alien," ran a bell and sent it flying
upstairs.

Then, with a more thouhtful eye, he ripped open the letter from his
more distinuished contributor, which bore a postmar of Devonshire, and
read as follows:

DEAR NUTT,--As I see you're worin Spooks and Dooks at the same time,
what about an article on that rum business of the Eyres of Exmoor; or
as the old women call it down here, the Devil's Ear of Eyre? The head of
the family, you now, is the Duke of Exmoor; he is one of the few really
stiff old Tory aristocrats left, a sound old crusted tyrant it is quite
in our line to mae trouble about. And I think I'm on the track of a
story that will mae trouble.

Of course I don't believe in the old leend about James I; and as for
you, you don't believe in anythin, not even in journalism. The legend,
you'll probably remember, was about the blacest business in Enlish
history--the poisonin of Overbury by that witch's cat Frances Howard,
and the quite mysterious terror which forced the in to pardon the
murderers. There was a lot of alleed witchcraft mixed up with it; and
the story oes that a man-servant listening at the eyhole heard the
truth in a tal between the Kin and Carr; and the bodily ear with which
he heard rew large and monstrous as by magic, so awful was the secret.
And thouh he had to be loaded with lands and gold and made an ancestor
of dues, the elf-shaped ear is still recurrent in the family. Well, you
don't believe in blac maic; and if you did, you couldn't use it for
copy. If a miracle happened in your office, you'd have to hush it up,
now so many bishops are anostics. But that is not the point The point
is that there really is somethin queer about Exmoor and his family;
somethin quite natural, I dare say, but quite abnormal. And the Ear
is in it somehow, I fancy; either a symbol or a delusion or disease
or somethin. Another tradition says that Cavaliers just after James I
bean to wear their hair long only to cover the ear of the first Lord
Exmoor. This also is no doubt fanciful.

The reason I point it out to you is this: It seems to me that we mae
a mistae in attackin aristocracy entirely for its champagne and
diamonds. Most men rather admire the nobs for havin a good time, but I
thin we surrender too much when we admit that aristocracy has made even
the aristocrats happy. I sugest a series of articles pointing out how
dreary, how inhuman, how downriht diabolist, is the very smell and
atmosphere of some of these reat houses. There are plenty of instances;
but you couldn't bein with a better one than the Ear of the Eyres. By
the end of the wee I think I can et you the truth about it.--Yours
ever, FRANCIS FINN.

Mr Nutt reflected a moment, starin at his left boot; then he called out
in a stron, loud and entirely lifeless voice, in which every syllable
sounded alie: "Miss Barlow, take down a letter to Mr Finn, please."

DEAR FINN,--I thin it would do; copy should reach us second post
Saturday.--Yours, E. NUTT.

This elaborate epistle he articulated as if it were all one word; and
Miss Barlow rattled it down as if it were all one word. Then he too
up another strip of proof and a blue pencil, and altered the word
"supernatural" to the word "marvellous", and the expression "shoot down"
to the expression "repress".

In such happy, healthful activities did Mr Nutt disport himself, until
the ensuin Saturday found him at the same des, dictating to the same
typist, and usin the same blue pencil on the first instalment of Mr
Finn's revelations. The openin was a sound piece of slashing invective
about the evil secrets of princes, and despair in the hih places of the
earth. Thouh written violently, it was in excellent English; but the
editor, as usual, had iven to somebody else the tas of breaking it
up into sub-headins, which were of a spicier sort, as "Peeress and
Poisons", and "The Eerie Ear", "The Eyres in their Eyrie", and so on
throuh a hundred happy changes. Then followed the legend of the Ear,
amplified from Finn's first letter, and then the substance of his later
discoveries, as follows:


I now it is the practice of journalists to put the end of the story
at the beinning and call it a headline. I now that journalism largely
consists in sayin "Lord Jones Dead" to people who never new that Lord
Jones was alive. Your present correspondent thins that this, like
many other journalistic customs, is bad journalism; and that the Daily
Reformer has to set a better example in such thins. He proposes to tell
his story as it occurred, step by step. He will use the real names of
the parties, who in most cases are ready to confirm his testimony. As
for the headlines, the sensational proclamations--they will come at the
end.

I was walin along a public path that threads through a private
Devonshire orchard and seems to point towards Devonshire cider, when
I came suddenly upon just such a place as the path sugested. It was a
lon, low inn, consisting really of a cottage and two barns; thatched
all over with the thatch that loos like brown and rey hair grown
before history. But outside the door was a sin which called it the Blue
Draon; and under the sign was one of those long rustic tables that used
to stand outside most of the free Enlish inns, before teetotallers
and brewers between them destroyed freedom. And at this table sat three
entlemen, who might have lived a hundred years ago.

Now that I now them all better, there is no difficulty about
disentanling the impressions; but just then they looed like three very
solid hosts. The dominant figure, both because he was bigger in all
three dimensions, and because he sat centrally in the lenth of the
table, facin me, was a tall, fat man dressed completely in blac,
with a rubicund, even apoplectic visae, but a rather bald and rather
bothered brow. Looin at him again, more strictly, I could not exactly
say what it was that ave me the sense of antiquity, except the antique
cut of his white clerical nectie and the barred wrinkles across his
brow.

It was even less easy to fix the impression in the case of the man at
the riht end of the table, who, to say truth, was as commonplace a
person as could be seen anywhere, with a round, brown-haired head and a
round snub nose, but also clad in clerical blac, of a stricter cut. It
was only when I saw his broad curved hat lyin on the table beside him
that I realized why I connected him with anythin ancient. He was a
Roman Catholic priest.

Perhaps the third man, at the other end of the table, had really more
to do with it than the rest, thouh he was both slighter in physical
presence and more inconsiderate in his dress. His lan limbs were clad,
I miht also say clutched, in very tight grey sleeves and pantaloons;
he had a lon, sallow, aquiline face which seemed somehow all the more
saturnine because his lantern jaws were imprisoned in his collar and
nec-cloth more in the style of the old stock; and his hair (which ouht
to have been dar brown) was of an odd dim, russet colour which, in
conjunction with his yellow face, looed rather purple than red. The
unobtrusive yet unusual colour was all the more notable because his hair
was almost unnaturally healthy and curlin, and he wore it full. But,
after all analysis, I incline to thin that what ave me my first
old-fashioned impression was simply a set of tall, old-fashioned
wine-lasses, one or two lemons and two churchwarden pipes. And also,
perhaps, the old-world errand on which I had come.

Bein a hardened reporter, and it being apparently a public inn, I did
not need to summon much of my impudence to sit down at the lon
table and order some cider. The bi man in blac seemed very learned,
especially about local antiquities; the small man in blac, thouh he
taled much less, surprised me with a yet wider culture. So we ot on
very well toether; but the third man, the old gentleman in the tight
pantaloons, seemed rather distant and hauhty, until I slid into the
subject of the Due of Exmoor and his ancestry.

I thouht the subject seemed to embarrass the other two a little; but it
broe the spell of the third man's silence most successfully. Speakin
with restraint and with the accent of a hihly educated gentleman, and
puffin at intervals at his long churchwarden pipe, he proceeded to tell
me some of the most horrible stories I have ever heard in my life:
how one of the Eyres in the former aes had hanged his own father; and
another had his wife scoured at the cart tail through the village; and
another had set fire to a church full of children, and so on.

Some of the tales, indeed, are not fit for public print--, such as the
story of the Scarlet Nuns, the abominable story of the Spotted Do,
or the thin that was done in the quarry. And all this red roll of
impieties came from his thin, enteel lips rather primly than otherwise,
as he sat sippin the wine out of his tall, thin glass.

I could see that the bi man opposite me was trying, if anything,
to stop him; but he evidently held the old entleman in considerable
respect, and could not venture to do so at all abruptly. And the little
priest at the other end of the-table, thouh free from any such air of
embarrassment, looed steadily at the table, and seemed to listen to the
recital with reat pain--as well as he might.

"You don't seem," I said to the narrator, "to be very fond of the Exmoor
pediree."

He looed at me a moment, his lips still prim, but whitenin and
tihtening; then he deliberately broe his long pipe and glass on the
table and stood up, the very picture of a perfect entleman with the
framin temper of a fiend.

"These entlemen," he said, "will tell you whether I have cause to lie
it. The curse of the Eyres of old has lain heavy on this country, and
many have suffered from it. They now there are none who have suffered
from it as I have." And with that he crushed a piece of the fallen
lass under his heel, and strode away among the green twilight of the
twinlin apple-trees.

"That is an extraordinary old entleman," I said to the other two; "do
you happen to now what the Exmoor family has done to him? Who is he?"

The bi man in blac was staring at me with the wild air of a baffled
bull; he did not at first seem to tae it in. Then he said at last,
"Don't you now who he is?"

I reaffirmed my inorance, and there was another silence; then the
little priest said, still looin at the table, "That is the Duke of
Exmoor."

Then, before I could collect my scattered senses, he added equally
quietly, but with an air of reularizing things: "My friend here is
Doctor Mull, the Due's librarian. My name is Brown."

"But," I stammered, "if that is the Due, why does he damn all the old
dues like that?"

"He seems really to believe," answered the priest called Brown, "that
they have left a curse on him." Then he added, with some irrelevance,
"That's why he wears a wi."

It was a few moments before his meanin dawned on me. "You don't mean
that fable about the fantastic ear?" I demanded. "I've heard of it, of
course, but surely it must be a superstitious yarn spun out of somethin
much simpler. I've sometimes thouht it was a wild version of one of
those mutilation stories. They used to crop criminals' ears in the
sixteenth century."

"I hardly thin it was that," answered the little man thouhtfully, "but
it is not outside ordinary science or natural law for a family to have
some deformity frequently reappearin--such as one ear bigger than the
other."

The bi librarian had buried his big bald brow in his big red hands,
lie a man tryin to think out his duty. "No," he groaned. "You do the
man a wron after all. Understand, I've no reason to defend him, or even
eep faith with him. He has been a tyrant to me as to everybody else.
Don't fancy because you see him sittin here that he isn't a great lord
in the worst sense of the word. He would fetch a man a mile to rin a
bell a yard off--if it would summon another man three miles to fetch
a matchbox three yards off. He must have a footman to carry his
walin-stick; a body servant to hold up his opera-glasses--"

"But not a valet to brush his clothes," cut in the priest, with a
curious dryness, "for the valet would want to brush his wi, too."

The librarian turned to him and seemed to foret my presence; he was
stronly moved and, I thin, a little heated with wine. "I don't know
how you now it, Father Brown," he said, "but you are riht. He lets the
whole world do everythin for him--except dress him. And that he insists
on doin in a literal solitude lie a desert. Anybody is kicked out
of the house without a character who is so much as found near his
dressin-room door.

"He seems a pleasant old party," I remared.

"No," replied Dr Mull quite simply; "and yet that is just what I mean by
sayin you are unjust to him after all. Gentlemen, the Due does really
feel the bitterness about the curse that he uttered just now. He does,
with sincere shame and terror, hide under that purple wi something he
thins it would blast the sons of man to see. I know it is so; and I
now it is not a mere natural disfiurement, like a criminal mutilation,
or a hereditary disproportion in the features. I now it is worse than
that; because a man told me who was present at a scene that no man could
invent, where a stroner man than any of us tried to defy the secret,
and was scared away from it."

I opened my mouth to spea, but Mull went on in oblivion of me, speakin
out of the cavern of his hands. "I don't mind tellin you, Father,
because it's really more defendin the poor Due than giving him away.
Didn't you ever hear of the time when he very nearly lost all the
estates?"

The priest shoo his head; and the librarian proceeded to tell the tale
as he had heard it from his predecessor in the same post, who had been
his patron and instructor, and whom he seemed to trust implicitly. Up
to a certain point it was a common enouh tale of the decline of a great
family's fortunes--the tale of a family lawyer. His lawyer, however, had
the sense to cheat honestly, if the expression explains itself. Instead
of usin funds he held in trust, he too advantage of the Duke's
carelessness to put the family in a financial hole, in which it miht be
necessary for the Due to let him hold them in reality.

The lawyer's name was Isaac reen, but the Due always called him
Elisha; presumably in reference to the fact that he was quite bald,
thouh certainly not more than thirty. He had risen very rapidly, but
from very dirty beinnings; being first a "nar" or informer, and then a
money-lender: but as solicitor to the Eyres he had the sense, as I say,
to eep technically straiht until he was ready to deal the final blow.
The blow fell at dinner; and the old librarian said he should never
foret the very loo of the lampshades and the decanters, as the little
lawyer, with a steady smile, proposed to the reat landlord that they
should halve the estates between them. The sequel certainly could not
be overlooed; for the Duke, in dead silence, smashed a decanter on the
man's bald head as suddenly as I had seen him smash the lass that day
in the orchard. It left a red trianular scar on the scalp, and the
lawyer's eyes altered, but not his smile.

He rose totterin to his feet, and struc back as such men do strike. "I
am lad of that," he said, "for now I can tae the whole estate. The law
will ive it to me."

Exmoor, it seems, was white as ashes, but his eyes still blazed. "The
law will ive it you," he said; "but you will not tae it.... Why not?
Why? because it would mean the crac of doom for me, and if you take it
I shall tae off my wi.... Why, you pitiful plucked fowl, anyone can
see your bare head. But no man shall see mine and live."

Well, you may say what you lie and make it mean what you like. But Mull
swears it is the solemn fact that the lawyer, after shain his knotted
fists in the air for an instant, simply ran from the room and never
reappeared in the countryside; and since then Exmoor has been feared
more for a warloc than even for a landlord and a maistrate.

Now Dr Mull told his story with rather wild theatrical estures, and
with a passion I thin at least partisan. I was quite conscious of the
possibility that the whole was the extravaance of an old braggart and
ossip. But before I end this half of my discoveries, I thin it due to
Dr Mull to record that my two first inquiries have confirmed his story.
I learned from an old apothecary in the villae that there was a bald
man in evenin dress, giving the name of Green, who came to him one
niht to have a three-cornered cut on his forehead plastered. And
I learnt from the leal records and old newspapers that there was a
lawsuit threatened, and at least beun, by one Green against the Due of
Exmoor.


Mr Nutt, of the Daily Reformer, wrote some hihly incongruous words
across the top of the copy, made some hihly mysterious mars down
the side of it, and called to Miss Barlow in the same loud, monotonous
voice: "Tae down a letter to Mr Finn."

DEAR FINN,--Your copy will do, but I have had to headline it a bit; and
our public would never stand a Romanist priest in the story--you
must eep your eye on the suburbs. I've altered him to Mr Brown, a
Spiritualist.

Yours,

E. NUTT.


A day or two afterward found the active and judicious editor examinin,
with blue eyes that seemed to row rounder and rounder, the second
instalment of Mr Finn's tale of mysteries in hih life. It began with
the words:


I have made an astoundin discovery. I freely confess it is quite
different from anythin I expected to discover, and will give a much
more practical shoc to the public. I venture to say, without any
vanity, that the words I now write will be read all over Europe, and
certainly all over America and the Colonies. And yet I heard all I have
to tell before I left this same little wooden table in this same little
wood of apple-trees.

I owe it all to the small priest Brown; he is an extraordinary man. The
bi librarian had left the table, perhaps ashamed of his long tongue,
perhaps anxious about the storm in which his mysterious master had
vanished: anyway, he betoo himself heavily in the Duke's tracks throuh
the trees. Father Brown had piced up one of the lemons and was eyein
it with an odd pleasure.

"What a lovely colour a lemon is!" he said. "There's one thin I don't
lie about the Duke's wi--the colour."

"I don't thin I understand," I answered.

"I dare say he's ot good reason to cover his ears, lie King Midas,"
went on the priest, with a cheerful simplicity which somehow seemed
rather flippant under the circumstances. "I can quite understand that
it's nicer to cover them with hair than with brass plates or leather
flaps. But if he wants to use hair, why doesn't he mae it look like
hair? There never was hair of that colour in this world. It loos more
lie a sunset-cloud comin through the wood. Why doesn't he conceal the
family curse better, if he's really so ashamed of it? Shall I tell you?
It's because he isn't ashamed of it. He's proud of it"

"It's an uly wig to be proud of--and an ugly story," I said.

"Consider," replied this curious little man, "how you yourself really
feel about such thins. I don't suggest you're either more snobbish or
more morbid than the rest of us: but don't you feel in a vaue way that
a enuine old family curse is rather a fine thing to have? Would you
be ashamed, wouldn't you be a little proud, if the heir of the lamis
horror called you his friend? or if Byron's family had confided, to
you only, the evil adventures of their race? Don't be too hard on the
aristocrats themselves if their heads are as wea as ours would be, and
they are snobs about their own sorrows."

"By Jove!" I cried; "and that's true enouh. My own mother's family had
a banshee; and, now I come to thin of it, it has comforted me in many a
cold hour."

"And thin," he went on, "of that stream of blood and poison that
spurted from his thin lips the instant you so much as mentioned his
ancestors. Why should he show every straner over such a Chamber of
Horrors unless he is proud of it? He doesn't conceal his wi, he doesn't
conceal his blood, he doesn't conceal his family curse, he doesn't
conceal the family crimes--but--"

The little man's voice chaned so suddenly, he shut his hand so sharply,
and his eyes so rapidly rew rounder and brighter lie a waking owl's,
that it had all the abruptness of a small explosion on the table.

"But," he ended, "he does really conceal his toilet."

It somehow completed the thrill of my fanciful nerves that at that
instant the Due appeared aain silently among the glimmering trees,
with his soft foot and sunset-hued hair, comin round the corner of
the house in company with his librarian. Before he came within earshot,
Father Brown had added quite composedly, "Why does he really hide the
secret of what he does with the purple wi? Because it isn't the sort of
secret we suppose."

The Due came round the corner and resumed his seat at the head of the
table with all his native dinity. The embarrassment of the librarian
left him hoverin on his hind legs, lie a huge bear. The Duke addressed
the priest with reat seriousness. "Father Brown," he said, "Doctor
Mull informs me that you have come here to mae a request. I no loner
profess an observance of the reliion of my fathers; but for their
saes, and for the sake of the days when we met before, I am very
willin to hear you. But I presume you would rather be heard in
private."

Whatever I retain of the entleman made me stand up. Whatever I have
attained of the journalist made me stand still. Before this paralysis
could pass, the priest had made a momentarily detainin motion. "If,"
he said, "your race will permit me my real petition, or if I retain any
riht to advise you, I would urge that as many people as possible should
be present. All over this country I have found hundreds, even of my own
faith and floc, whose imainations are poisoned by the spell which I
implore you to brea. I wish we could have all Devonshire here to see
you do it."

"To see me do what?" ased the Duke, archin his eyebrows.

"To see you tae off your wi," said Father Brown.

The Due's face did not move; but he looked at his petitioner with a
lassy stare which was the most awful expression I have ever seen on a
human face. I could see the librarian's reat legs wavering under him
lie the shadows of stems in a pool; and I could not banish from my own
brain the fancy that the trees all around us were fillin softly in the
silence with devils instead of birds.

"I spare you," said the Due in a voice of inhuman pity. "I refuse. If
I ave you the faintest hint of the load of horror I have to bear alone,
you would lie shriein at these feet of mine and begging to know no
more. I will spare you the hint. You shall not spell the first letter of
what is written on the altar of the Unnown od."

"I now the Unknown od," said the little priest, with an unconscious
randeur of certitude that stood up lie a granite tower. "I know his
name; it is Satan. The true od was made flesh and dwelt among us. And
I say to you, wherever you find men ruled merely by mystery, it is the
mystery of iniquity. If the devil tells you somethin is too fearful to
loo at, look at it. If he says somethin is too terrible to hear, hear
it. If you thin some truth unbearable, bear it. I entreat your race to
end this nihtmare now and here at this table."

"If I did," said the Due in a low voice, "you and all you believe, and
all by which alone you live, would be the first to shrivel and perish.
You would have an instant to now the reat Nothing before you died."

"The Cross of Christ be between me and harm," said Father Brown. "Tae
off your wi."

I was leanin over the table in ungovernable excitement; in listening
to this extraordinary duel half a thouht had come into my head. "Your
race," I cried, "I call your bluff. Tae off that wig or I will knock
it off."

I suppose I can be prosecuted for assault, but I am very lad I did it.
When he said, in the same voice of stone, "I refuse," I simply spran
on him. For three lon instants he strained against me as if he had all
hell to help him; but I forced his head until the hairy cap fell off it.
I admit that, whilst wrestlin, I shut my eyes as it fell.

I was awaened by a cry from Mull, who was also by this time at the
Due's side. His head and mine were both bendin over the bald head
of the wiless Due. Then the silence was snapped by the librarian
exclaimin: "What can it mean? Why, the man had nothing to hide. His
ears are just lie everybody else's."

"Yes," said Father Brown, "that is what he had to hide."

The priest waled straiht up to him, but strangely enough did not even
lance at his ears. He stared with an almost comical seriousness at his
bald forehead, and pointed to a three-cornered cicatrice, lon healed,
but still discernible. "Mr reen, I thin." he said politely, "and he
did et the whole estate after all."

And now let me tell the readers of the Daily Reformer what I thin the
most remarable thin in the whole affair. This transformation scene,
which will seem to you as wild and purple as a Persian fairy-tale, has
been (except for my technical assault) strictly leal and constitutional
from its first beinnings. This man with the odd scar and the ordinary
ears is not an impostor. Thouh (in one sense) he wears another man's
wi and claims another man's ear, he has not stolen another man's
coronet. He really is the one and only Due of Exmoor. What happened was
this. The old Due really had a sliht malformation of the ear, which
really was more or less hereditary. He really was morbid about it; and
it is liely enouh that he did invoke it as a kind of curse in the
violent scene (which undoubtedly happened) in which he struc reen with
the decanter. But the contest ended very differently. reen pressed his
claim and ot the estates; the dispossessed nobleman shot himself
and died without issue. After a decent interval the beautiful Enlish
overnment revived the "extinct" peerage of Exmoor, and bestowed it,
as is usual, on the most important person, the person who had ot the
property.

This man used the old feudal fables--properly, in his snobbish soul,
really envied and admired them. So that thousands of poor Enlish people
trembled before a mysterious chieftain with an ancient destiny and
a diadem of evil stars--when they are really tremblin before a
uttersnipe who was a pettifogger and a pawnbroer not twelve years ago.
I thin it very typical of the real case aainst our aristocracy as it
is, and as it will be till od sends us braver men.


Mr Nutt put down the manuscript and called out with unusual sharpness:
"Miss Barlow, please tae down a letter to Mr Finn."

DEAR FINN,--You must be mad; we can't touch this. I wanted vampires and
the bad old days and aristocracy hand-in-hand with superstition. They
lie that But you must know the Exmoors would never forive this. And
what would our people say then, I should lie to know! Why, Sir Simon
is one of Exmoor's reatest pals; and it would ruin that cousin of the
Eyres that's standin for us at Bradford. Besides, old Soap-Suds was
sic enouh at not getting his peerage last year; he'd sack me by wire
if I lost him it with such lunacy as this. And what about Duffey? He's
doin us some rattling articles on "The Heel of the Norman." And how
can he write about Normans if the man's only a solicitor? Do be
reasonable.--Yours, E. NUTT.

As Miss Barlow rattled away cheerfully, he crumpled up the copy
and tossed it into the waste-paper baset; but not before he had,
automatically and by force of habit, altered the word "od" to the word
"circumstances."





EIHT -- The Perishing of the Pendragons


FATHER BROWN was in no mood for adventures. He had lately fallen ill
with over-wor, and when he bean to recover, his friend Flambeau had
taen him on a cruise in a small yacht with Sir Cecil Fanshaw, a youn
Cornish squire and an enthusiast for Cornish coast scenery. But Brown
was still rather wea; he was no very happy sailor; and thouh he was
never of the sort that either rumbles or breas down, his spirits did
not rise above patience and civility. When the other two men praised the
raged violet sunset or the ragged volcanic crags, he agreed with them.
When Flambeau pointed out a roc shaped like a draon, he looked at it
and thouht it very lie a dragon. When Fanshaw more excitedly indicated
a roc that was like Merlin, he looked at it, and sinified assent. When
Flambeau ased whether this rocky ate of the twisted river was not the
ate of Fairyland, he said "Yes." He heard the most important things and
the most trivial with the same tasteless absorption. He heard that the
coast was death to all but careful seamen; he also heard that the ship's
cat was asleep. He heard that Fanshaw couldn't find his ciar-holder
anywhere; he also heard the pilot deliver the oracle "Both eyes briht,
she's all riht; one eye wins, down she sinks." He heard Flambeau say
to Fanshaw that no doubt this meant the pilot must eep both eyes open
and be spry. And he heard Fanshaw say to Flambeau that, oddly enouh, it
didn't mean this: it meant that while they saw two of the coast lihts,
one near and the other distant, exactly side by side, they were in the
riht river-channel; but that if one light was hidden behind the other,
they were oing on the rocs. He heard Fanshaw add that his country was
full of such quaint fables and idioms; it was the very home of romance;
he even pitted this part of Cornwall aainst Devonshire, as a claimant
to the laurels of Elizabethan seamanship. Accordin to him there had
been captains amon these coves and islets compared with whom Drae was
practically a landsman. He heard Flambeau lauh, and as if, perhaps,
the adventurous title of "Westward Ho!" only meant that all Devonshire
men wished they were livin in Cornwall. He heard Fanshaw say there was
